The Best Medicine

by Cassie_Bones

Summary

Agatha Harkness just received some devastating news that sends her into a depression that she can't quite seem to get herself out of, even with the help of her oldest and dearest friend, Jen Kale.

So, on the advice of her doctor, Agatha attends a support group for women with her same illness. There, she meets Rio Vidal, a self-described "sit down comedian" who has a mischievous gleam in her eyes and an obvious interest in Agatha herself.

Agatha can't deny that she feels the spark there, too, but she doesn't know if now is the right time to start something with somebody, no matter how much chemistry they seem to have.

Notes

I've been thinking about writing this one for a while. There's not enough chronic illness rep in this (or many other) fandom. So I'm injecting a little bit of my own story to this. I hope you like it and, if you feel you can relate specifically to Agatha's struggle, I hope you know that you are seen and supported and definitely not alone.

See the end of the work for more notes

Chapter 1: FIve Senses

Chapter One

Her grandmother had always told her to focus on her five senses when she was nervous, so that’s what Agatha did as she sat waiting for the two minutes it took the doctor to enter the exam room.

Hear. She could hear the ticking of the clock over the door. It was off by about thirty seconds. Agatha’s time changed before the minute hand dragged itself across the blocky twelve at the top of the clock.

Smell . It smelled like rubbing alcohol in this room, sterile in a way that tickled her nose. It didn’t burn her eyes, though, which was a plus. 

See . She saw a lot of things. The walls in this room were light blue, probably in an attempt to comfort the patients. It was hard to be comforted in a place like this, though; this medical center was for a specific purpose. Nobody actually wanted to be in a place like this, she was certain. An infographic poster on the wall assured her that she was not alone, as three in four people diagnosed with Multiple Sclerosis were female. 

But Agatha Harkness had not yet been diagnosed, so there was still hope that she was here by accident. There was still hope that the MRI that had caught the white matter in her brain was a fluke. There was still hope that the doctor would walk into the room and apologize because this had all been a huge misunderstanding. Agatha Harkness did not have MS. She was a runner and an artist, a painter. The numbness that she had felt in her left side just a year ago was actually caused by the flu shot she’d received two weeks prior. It had returned to normal just two weeks after that, after all, after the ER doctors and her own primary care physician sent her to the neurologist just to be sure.

Agatha had been dumb about the MRI order, she knew. She should have gotten it right away. But by the time she saw the neurologist with the gentle voice and the kind eyes, she had regained all feeling in her left hand. She could write and paint again with ease. She wasn’t stumbling over herself anymore. She was fine .

She’d found the MRI order in a desk drawer in the summer, nearly nine months after it had been written up for her. It was good for up to a year. Agatha didn’t think it would yield any results, but she figured it couldn’t hurt just to schedule it.

She was obviously wrong.

Taste . She could still taste her coffee from that morning. She’d been out of oatmilk, so she had to drink it black. She tried to sweeten it with a couple packets of cane sugar she’d stolen from Dunkin, but it didn’t do much to take the bitter taste out of her mouth. 

She really needed to stop drinking coffee: it made her anxiety infinitely worse.

Feel . The exam bed underneath her was uncomfortable, the paper crinkling with every move she made. Agatha hated sitting still. She’d always hated it. She was a runner. She needed to run. It helped clear her mind, as if she could leave any and all thoughts behind her pounding feet on the pavement. She ran nearly every morning before dawn, pushing herself further and further and further…

Would she still be able to do that?

The door opened and she jumped slightly, looking up as an older woman with salt and pepper hair entered, a pair of glasses slipping down her nose. Her eyes were brown-hazel and her smile was kind. She sat down on the little moving stool, pushing herself towards Agatha.

“Hello,” she said, “I’m Doctor Calderu. You must be Agatha Harkness.” She had a subtle Italian lilt to her accent. Agatha offered her a stiff smile, reaching out to shake her hand. “How are you doing today?” Dr. Calderu asked, tilting her head in genuine interest.

Agatha shrugged. “Honestly,” she said, “I’ve been better.”

Dr. Calderu chuckled at that. “I bet,” she said. “It’s always a scary thing to be faced with a serious illness, but it doesn’t have to be.”

“I dunno,” Agatha said, looking down at her jean-clad knees. “MS seems like a pretty… devastating disease to possibly have. As far as I’ve seen, anyway.”

“It can be,” Dr. Calderu admitted. “But it doesn’t have to be. You seem rather physically fit, and your MRI scans aren’t too concerning.”

“What does that even mean?” Agatha sighed. “Aren’t there lesions in my brain?”

“Yes,” Dr. Calderu confirmed, “but they are new. This indicates that the disease may be at its earliest stages.”

“So I do have it, then?” Agatha felt her heart stutter in her chest, her breathing growing shallow.

“It is indicated by your scans and other tests, yes,” Dr. Calderu said, “but I would like to perform a few more just to be certain. Is that okay?” Agatha nodded, fighting past the lump in her throat. “Okay,” the doctor said. She lifted her hands, palms facing Agatha. “Please push my hands away from you.”

Agatha lifted her hands and pushed Dr. Calderu’s away. Her hands were slender and soft. The doctor gave her a smile as Agatha easily pushed her hands away despite the doctor’s resisting effort.

“Good,” she said, placing her fingers together as her palms now faced herself. “Now pull them towards you.” 

Agatha tugged Dr. Calderu’s hands towards herself, and the doctor nodded.

“Good,” she said. “A few more tests.”

“I’ve already done these,” Agatha complained as the doctor instructed her to put her own hands up and resist her trying to push and pull them.

“But not with me,” Dr. Calderu said. “And when you did these tests with your other doctors, they were not looking for signs of MS, but rather for signs of stroke, which you thankfully did not have. Push up.” She placed her hand on Agatha’s knee, and Agatha lifted it against her palm. She knew that her legs were strong, muscular from her daily run. “Good,” she said. “Now stand and do as I say.”

Dr. Calderu had Agatha walk on her tiptoes, then on her heels, and Agatha stumbled a little bit, her face warming and her heart jumping. Was this a sign of MS? Could people without the disease do this kind of thing with ease? 

She then had Agatha walk with her heel touching the toes of the other foot. This was also a bit of a challenge, but Agatha didn’t stumble at all this time. 

“Good,” Dr. Calderu said, writing something down on her clipboard. “Sit back down and take off your shoes for me.” 

Agatha had worn her Converse for the appointment. They weren’t her usual running shoes, but she liked the dark purple color and how they felt on her feet. Plus, they were easy to toe off.

“Socks, too,” the doctor requested, smiling at the Starry Night -inspired socks. Agatha toed them off as well, tucking them into her sneakers. 

Dr. Calderu pulled out a tuning fork from the pocket of her lab coat. “I’m going to test the reflexes in your feet,” she explained. “I want you to look away while I do this. When you can no longer feel the vibrations, let me know. I will do this first on the right foot, then the left. Understand?” Agatha nodded. “Good. Look away.”

Agatha looked at the wall that had the infographic on it. She read it a couple times as she felt the vibrations in her foot slowly fade until she couldn’t feel anything anymore.

“Okay,” she said, turning back to the doctor, who nodded, writing something on her clipboard.

“Next foot,” Dr. Calderu, motioning for Agatha to turn away. 

This time, Agatha looked at the blank baby blue-colored wall. It was slightly textured, the paint job looking relatively new. She couldn’t smell the paint, though, so it couldn’t have been that new. 

When the buzzing in her left foot stopped, she said, “Okay” and looked back at the doctor again. The doctor nodded.

“Five seconds,” she said. “That is the difference between the time your right foot stops buzzing and your left foot.”

“Is that…significant?” Agatha asked.

Dr. Calderu stared at her, smiling, for five full seconds before she answered: “Yes.” Agatha nodded in understanding, her chest feeling tight again. “ But ,” the doctor continued, “it confirms my suspicions that your MS is in the very early stages. So long as we start treatment immediately, you should be perfectly fine.”

“Except for the fact that my central nervous system is going to be slowly shutting down for the rest of my life, you mean?” Agatha huffed, frowning.

“I see you have been Googling ,” Dr. Calderu chuckled. “That is fine. But you need to take everything you read on the internet with a grain of salt. Everybody’s experience is different, and treatment will slow the progression of the disease. You are lucky to have found this out now.”

“I don’t feel very lucky,” Agatha murmured, looking down at her feet.

“Miss Harkness…” 

“Agatha,” Agatha corrected. She always hated being called by her last name. It connected her too much to her mother.

“Agatha,” Dr. Calderu echoed. “You are lucky. Not to have developed this disease, of course, but that it was caught so early. This is almost unheard of . More often than not, I see patients who have already lost feeling in one or both of their legs, who need canes or even wheelchairs , who cannot walk more than twenty feet without losing their breath. So believe me when I say that you are lucky. Now that we’ve caught the MS, we can begin treatment and slow it down to a snail’s pace. I can’t promise you that you’ll never have a need for a mobility aid of some sort, but I can promise you that it’s a long way off. Okay?”

Agatha nodded, tears still slipping down her cheeks as her heart continued to squeeze in her chest. Dr. Calderu handed her a tissue. Agatha blew her nose.

“How old are you?” the doctor asked.

“Twenty-Nine,” Agatha said, sniffling. “This shouldn’t be happening to me.”

“Listen,” Dr. Calderu said, “you have a long, long life ahead of you. Don’t let this make you feel as though you can’t do anything and everything you set your mind to. It will all be okay.” She reached out, taking Agatha’s hand. Her grip was stronger than the younger woman had expected it to be. It was comforting. “Do you have any family?” the doctor asked. “A support system at home?”

Agatha hesitated at that. Her father had died when she was two. Her mother had never been kind to her even before that. Her father’s mother had done most of the child-rearing after his passing, teaching Agatha everything she needed to know. Agatha had even gone to live with her when she was seven, staying with her until her gran’s death when she was twenty-one, when Agatha was still in undergrad. She’d left Agatha with enough money in her will to go to grad school for Art History, leaving her house to Agatha.

Agatha’s mother had attempted to take that house from her, but Agatha had fought back. Evanora Harkness had no claim to any of her mother-in-law’s assets, but that didn’t stop her from antagonizing Agatha until her own death when Agatha was twenty-seven. By that point, Agatha had earned her Master’s and opened an art gallery with an old classmate of hers, but she didn’t know if she’d count Jen Kale as family.

She was the closest thing she had, though.

Still, Agatha shook her head.

Dr. Calderu nodded and reached for one of the pamphlets from the counter, handing it to Agatha. “This is a support group,” she said, “for women with MS. They meet twice a week at the Westview Community Center. Please consider attending.”

Agatha nodded, taking the pamphlet, though she had no real intentions of going to a support group. She didn’t need anybody else. She had gotten through adult life thus far mostly on her own. She didn’t need anybody.

 

I don’t need anybody.

That was the mantra in her head as she changed into her running shorts and hoodie, slipping easily from her Converse to the running shoes, and headed off on her second run that day. 

It was a nice day for October, the sun still shining through the gaps in the trees as leaves crunched underneath Agatha’s feet, which pounded the pavement of the sidewalks through Westview. 

I don’t need anybody else. I’ll be fine. I am fine.

She passed the Westview Community Center without giving it a cursory glance, shaking her head slightly as she picked up her pace, checking for cars as she crossed the street, keeping her breathing even as she started running up an incline. She loved hills. They provided her with a challenge. They made her muscles work just a little bit harder, added a little bit of strain to her lungs and heart, more muscles that could be strengthened.

Will I still be able to do this in ten years? Twenty?

Agatha shook away the thoughts, her long ponytail slightly slapping her in the side of the face as she continued to run, sweat beading on her temples, starting to make the front of her shirt damp. 

She ran past a park with a track that she ran on in the mornings, when it was mostly empty. Now, there were people milling about, families having picnics in the afternoon sun, smiling and laughing like there wasn’t a worry in the entire world.

Agatha picked up her speed again, running straight past the park and taking a familiar turn at the corner. She knew this neighborhood – this whole town, really – like the back of her hand. She had moved to Westview, New Jersey, from Salem, Massachusetts, when she moved in with her gran, after her mother had signed away her rights, not giving a single shit about her only child. Agatha had been upset when Evanora told her to start packing her shit, that she was moving out. She had thought that Evanora was taking her away, moving her further from the grandmother that she didn’t get to see nearly enough because of her mother.

But then Gran had been in the kitchen when Agatha walked out with her suitcases, beaming down at her with her arms out. Agatha had run straight into them, pressing her face into her gran’s neck, feeling her kisses on her cheeks and temples.

“Let’s go home,” she’d said, taking one of Agatha’s suitcases and guiding her out the door with a gentle hand on her back. 

Agatha hadn’t even spared her mother a second glance.

She never missed Evanora, not even for a second. And Evanora never called, anyway. She did show up at her grandmother’s funeral, but Agatha hadn’t even acknowledged her, ignoring whispers about her being ‘cold’ and ‘cruel’ to her own mother. 

She didn’t give two fucks about Evanora Harkness. Agatha hadn’t even attended her funeral. When she’d been sent a small urn of her mother’s ashes by a distant family member, she’d poured them down the toilet because fuck that bitch .

Agatha’s calves were starting to burn with her effort. She had probably already run the entire length of Westview, which was confirmed when she saw the distant town sign in the distance. If she ran past it, it wouldn’t be too long before she reached Eastview.

She didn’t feel like going to Eastview today.

Agatha made a wide U-turn, crossing the street to keep herself from stopping. She couldn’t stop. If she stopped running, everything, she knew, would fall apart. She couldn’t let everything fall apart. Not yet.

She turned another corner, making her way back in the direction of her house. Her legs were starting to get tired. Usually, she could run right through it, push herself just a tiny bit more, but she had already done that today. She was just tired now. She needed to rest. And she needed to be somewhere safe when she broke down.

She didn’t run home.

Jennifer Kale lived two blocks down from Agatha, in one of the fancier apartment buildings on a whole block of apartment buildings. Her doorman instantly recognized Agatha as she ran toward him, opening the door for her just in time.

“Thanks, George!” Agatha called over her shoulder as she jogged through the lobby, bypassing the elevators in favor of the stairs for the third floor apartment.

Jen lived in Apartment 3B. Agatha rapped on the door, jogging in place as she waited for the door to open. Her heart was pounding in her throat now, her knees aching slightly, the muscles in her calves burning as she waited…

“Agatha?” Jen asked, placing a hand on her hip. She was wearing a salmon-colored sweater and a matching beanie. Her pants were soft gray cotton and her feet were bare on the hardwood floor. “What are you doing here?”

And, all at once, the dam burst. 

Tears flooded Agatha’s eyes the second she stopped jogging, her legs giving out beneath her as she crumpled to the ground, her body wracked with sobs. Jen’s eyes widened and she immediately got to the ground with her, her perfectly-manicured fingers reaching out to cup Agatha’s cheeks, which were drenched with her tears. 

“Agatha?” she asked, panicked. “Agatha!” 

Agatha leaned forward, pressing her face to Jen’s shoulder, her tears soaking through the impossibly soft sweater. All Jen could do was wrap her arms around Agatha’s shoulders, her hands stroking up and down her back. She had never seen Agatha shed more than a few tears over a particularly moving art piece. To see her break down like this over…well, Jennifer didn’t even know what had happened. What could have happened to bring Agatha Harkness to tears like this?

“Come on,” Jen said, tugging Agatha into the apartment and shutting the door. Agatha obviously wasn’t in her right mind because the Agatha that Jen knew – stone-cold, tough as nails, sharply charming, secretly soft and somewhat maternal – would never want a stranger to see her cry. The fact that she was here, sobbing in Jennifer’s arms right now, was…worrying. 

Jen led Agatha to her couch, which was light pink and plush and had a fuzzy white throw blanket on the back of it. Jen sat down with Agatha still in her arms and tugged the blanket around her shoulders, separating only for long enough to wrap Agatha up before wrapping her arms around her again. Agatha wasted no time in pressing her wet face to Jen’s neck again.

Jen just waited her out, rubbing Agatha’s shoulder and pressing her cheek to the top of her head. Agatha’s shoulders continued to shake for a few more minutes as she sobbed, but Jen could tell she was starting to wind down.

When all she could hear were a few watery sniffles and the clearing of Agatha’s throat, she finally pulled away some to look down into Agatha’s eyes. Agatha avoided her gaze, her cheeks pink with embarrassment, the hair that had escaped from her ponytail sticking to her face. Jen pushed it away, softly.

“Hey,” she said. 

Agatha still didn’t look at her.

“You want some water?” Jen asked, trying to keep her voice even and casual. “You look like you just went for a run; you’re going to dehydrate yourself if you keep leaving the house without water, dumbass.” She shoved Agatha’s shoulder playfully when Agatha let out an amused snort, nodding.

“Okay,” Jen said, standing up out of Agatha’s embrace. Agatha let her go easily, wrapping the blanket more firmly around herself as she sank into the couch. 

Jen spared her a concerned glance as she entered her kitchen area (thank Goddess for open floor plans) and grabbed a bottle of water from the fridge. She brought it back to Agatha, who was now staring blankly into outer space. Jen flopped down next to her, pressing the water into her hands.

Agatha’s fist closed around the bottle and she looked down at it. Her brows furrowed and she let out a scoff. “ Voss ?” she said. “Really, Kale? How pretentious.”

“And there she is,” Jen laughed. “Welcome back, Harkness.” She knocked against Agatha’s shoulder with her own. 

Agatha rolled her eyes, taking a long sip of her water. Jen waited her out for a moment or two before speaking again.

“So,” she said, “what happened?”

More tears appeared in Agatha’s eyes, but she stubbornly refused to let them fall. “I went to the doctor today.”

She hadn’t told Jen that she was going to the doctor. She had only told Jen about her left side going a little fuzzy. She was still able to use it, but she was clumsier, and it limited her ability to paint, so she had mostly ignored it, blaming it on the stupid flu shot, until she had something more to worry about.

Jen had forgotten about it, obviously.

“Okay,” she said. “Why? What’s going on? Is it about the headaches?”

Shit . Agatha had forgotten about the headaches. She should have told her doctor about them, too. It made sense that she’d have them, what with the lesions in her brain. 

She’d complained to Jen about them a few times, popping a few Advil every now and then to rid herself of them so that she could stand the bright lights in their gallery during shows. It usually helped.

Usually.

“Sort of…” she said. “It’s connected, I think.” She shrugged.

“Okay…do you have, like, a brain tumor?” Jen asked. “Is it brain tumor Agatha that’s a bitch? If they remove it, will you be all sunshine and rainbows?”

“Shut up,” Agatha huffed, but she couldn’t resist the upturn of her lips. “No,” she said. “I don’t have a brain tumor, Kale. Bitchy Agatha is real Agatha.”

“Shame,” Jen tutted. Agatha shoved her so hard that she fell over, cackling.

“Fuck you.”

“You’re not my type,” Jen retorted, pushing herself back up.

“I was in junior year,” Agatha snarked.

“And look how that turned out.”

“With a successful art gallery?” Agatha shrugged. “Could’ve gone worse.”

“ After you ghosted me for a full year until our graduation,” Jen pointed out. 

“I thought you said I wasn’t your type, Jenny,” Agatha purred, batting her eyelashes at the taller woman.

“You’re not ,” Jen huffed, shoving her. “Don’t call me Jenny. I left my favorite sweater in your dorm room, and it took forever to get it back because you kept dodging me.”

“You got it back eventually, didn’t you?” Agatha huffed. 

“Whatever,” Jen said, rolling her eyes. Then she got serious. “So…what happened?”

Agatha took another sip of water, steeling herself for what she was about to reveal. She swallowed past the lump in her throat and pressed her lips together before looking up at Jen, her oldest…friend? They were business partners, but she wouldn’t have considered Jen Kale her friend until right now.

Really, she was her only friend. 

Agatha got along well with the artists whose work they showcased, but she didn’t go out for drinks with any of them. She didn’t veg out on their couches on off days, watching bad, usually straight, rom coms and drinking wine like she did with Jen. They barely ever hugged. Jen hadn’t really seen Agatha cry until now. Agatha had never seen Jennifer cry.

But they were friends, Agatha realized. They were best friends, as odd as it felt to even think. As such, she decided that Jen deserved to know. After all, they also worked together. If something happened and Agatha couldn’t do that anymore, well Jen would be the one that deserved to know the most.

“I have MS,” Agatha blurted, the words sending a shockwave throughout her entire body. It was the first time she’d said those words aloud. It felt unreal, like they belonged to somebody else. They couldn’t possibly belong to Agatha, who ran miles every single morning before she even had her first cup of coffee. Agatha, who kept herself in shape and ate well enough and worked out whenever she had a spare hour to herself. Agatha, who worked hard to run a successful art gallery and sold her own paintings for more money than she would have ever thought possible.

How could that Agatha Harkness have such a devastating disease?

“I’m sorry to hear that,” Jen said, her voice low and gentle. “That really sucks, Agatha.”

Agatha nodded, tears falling down her cheeks again. She swiped them away furiously. “Yeah,” she said. “It really does.”

She felt Jen’s fingers interlace with hers between their bodies. She felt Jen’s cheek rest on top of her head again, closed her eyes as Jen’s warmth enveloped hers again, comforting her. Agatha leaned back against her shoulder, taking a deep, shuddering breath.

“Do you wanna talk about it?” Jen asked after a long moment of silence.

“No,” Agatha sniffled.

“Do you wanna order takeout and get drunk?”

Yeah, Jen Kale was definitely her best friend.

“Yes,” Agatha said, nodding. “ Please .”

“Okay,” Jen said. “I have a bottle of noir I’ve been meaning to crack open. Pizza, sushi, or thai?”

“I don’t care,” Agatha sighed. “Whatever you feel like is fine. Can I take a shower? I feel smelly.”

“You are smelly,” Jen snorted, dodging Agatha’s kick as she ran away from the couch, chortling. “Girl, you just went for a run. You showed up drenched in sweat. Even you can admit that you stank .” She waved her hand in front of her face as she went for the takeout drawer next to her fridge, pulling out an array of menus and spreading them out on the counter. “There are fresh towels in the hall closet,” she called over her shoulder as she Eeny Meeny Miney Mo’ed their options. “I’ll put some clean clothes outside the bathroom if you don’t want to get back into those sweaty ones. You can wash them while we eat.”

“Sounds good,” Agatha said, groaning as she got to her feet. Her legs felt like jelly after that run. Good thing Jennifer Kale was a pretentious priss who not only bought overpriced bottles of water, but had also renovated her shower so that there was a bench for Agatha to sit on as the hot water ran over her sore muscles.

She sat in the shower for far too long, allowing the water to rinse off the run and the rest of that day down the drain. She used Jennifer’s expensive, coconut-scented body wash and her pink sugar scrub on her legs, then wrapped herself in a hot pink towel, sighing in relief as she sat at the vanity in the oversized bathroom. 

Jennifer Kale had grown up the only child of two ridiculously wealthy and incredibly affectionate suburbanites. Her mother, Naomi, showed up for all holidays, major or minor (think Arbor Day) to spend time with Jen and, by extension, Agatha. She oohed and aahed at their art installations, raining affection and praise on ‘her’ girls, and treated them to nice dinners on the town.

Agatha actually adored Naomi Kale. She was everything that Agatha’s grandmother had been and everything Agatha wished that her mother would have been.

Being raised wealthy and well-loved, Jen was relatively well-rounded. She was the tiniest bit selfish (she didn’t share food and she was always her own priority), but she did have a generous spirit for the most part. She gave to charity and volunteered at some local events (which she insisted was just a way to get good press for the gallery) and, despite consistently teasing her, was always there for Agatha when she needed her most.

Like now.

Agatha slipped into a t-shirt and a pair of Jen’s sweatpants, which she had to roll the waistband several times not to trip over herself, and padded barefoot into the kitchen, where Jen was already unpacking several containers of sushi and rice.

“Of course you went for the most expensive option,” Agatha huffed even as she reached for a container of rice. “How much do I owe you?” They always split their shared meals fifty-fifty.

“Nothing,” Jen said. “This one’s on me. I’m keeping most of the leftovers, anyway. You can have your California and salmon rolls.” She wrinkled her nose, pushing two containers toward Agatha.

“Don’t make that face,” Agatha huffed. “They’re delicious .”

“They’re children’s sushi,” Jen said, pointing her chopsticks at Agatha. “You need to mature your palate.”

“With what?” Agatha snorted. “Spicy tuna rolls? Very mature.”

“Try some of my inari,” Jen said, holding up her container. “It’s delicious .”

Agatha wrinkled her nose, pulling away from the counter with her food and chopsticks. “No thanks,” she said. “I think I’ve been through enough today, don’t you?”

“Ugh,” Jen said. “Agatha Harkness has a new card to play.”

“Yep!” Agatha said, sticking her nose up as she walked into the living room, placing her food onto the coffee table. “And I am going to use it generously for a while, so be warned.”

“Noted,” Jen snorted, balancing her chopsticks and sushi in one hand, two wine glasses in the other, and her bottle of pinot noir under her arm. She expertly placed everything on her side of the coffee table and reached for the remote. “So,” she said, “what are we watching?”

Chapter 2: Exceptional

Chapter Summary

Agatha goes through some more stress related to her illness and starts treatment. A little more backstory here, too.

Chapter Notes

Thank you so much for all the positive feedback from my first chapter. I'm so glad that many of you connected with not only the characters but the subject matter. I'm doing fine right now. My MS is in the incredibly early stages, though I have faced some difficulties which will definitely be injected into this story.

For any and everybody facing a chronic illness, I support you and I hope you're doing well.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Chapter Two

Thankfully, Jen Kale’s bougie ass apartment also had a spare room with a queen-sized bed that was just as soft as one might expect. Agatha stretched across it, getting tangled up in the sheets, and was out like a light in minutes…

Only to wake up at three in the morning with her heart pounding, her face covered in sweat like she had just run several miles again . She sat up, shoving the blankets off of herself and pressed her hand to her chest, fighting to breathe as though somebody were covering her mouth and nose with their full fucking hand. She furiously shoved back the strands of hair that were sticking to her face, irritating her.

Agatha leaned forward, wrapping her arms around her knees, making herself as small as possible, tightening her entire body into a ball, trying to calm her racing heart and slow her breathing.

When she finally did, she felt her body relax slightly into the mattress and the pillows behind her, but she didn’t release herself from the ball. Instead, she fell sideways, still balled up, and shut her eyes tight, willing herself to go back to sleep even as tears started to squeeze out from beneath her tightly shut eyes. 

“Please,” she whispered, sniffling. “Please, please, please…”

She willed herself to fall asleep or, maybe, to wake up. Surely, this was the bad dream. This nightmare where she was diagnosed with a debilitating disease that would slowly take her fine motor functions away, leaving her with that fuzzy feeling from the year prior, which had rendered her dominant hand almost entirely useless.

She needed to wake up from this dream. Maybe she’d wake up in her own bed, two blocks away, to the blaring of her alarm that reminded her to go for her daily run. Maybe the lingering ache in her legs from her last run would diminish and she’d be able to get up and get ready for the next one. Maybe she’d attend the real doctor’s visit and be told that it was all just a mistake: her MRI scans were clear and perfect and showed that Agatha was actually the healthiest person they’d ever seen, mentally, physically, and emotionally.

Okay, even that last part was a stretch.

Agatha had seen a therapist in her late teens, at her gran’s insistence. Dr. Hart had attributed everything to childhood trauma, more or less. The nightmares she’d been having about her mother showing up to take her away (impossible, as Gran had been her legal guardian for nearly a decade at that point) to her love of art and the emotions it invoked in Agatha which were otherwise hard for her to really show. 

“Art is the key to your heart, ” Dr. Hart had said. “It unlocks something deep within you, something that you can’t quite unlock on your own. ”

She was a quack, Agatha decided, and Agatha convinced Gran to let her stop going after three months, insisting that her nightmares were over (they were not, but Agatha was better at dealing with them at that point, no longer screaming when she awoke) and that the therapist was weird and way too perky.

And she didn’t like her nasally laugh.

Gran had relented, warning Agatha that if she woke her up with one more nightmare, she would be sent to a new therapist. Agatha had agreed to this, but she never screamed in her sleep again, able to keep herself quiet if she ever woke up crying.

Also, Gran didn’t like to admit it, but she was in serious need of some hearing aids, so Agatha doubted she’d hear very much anyway .

At some point, Agatha drifted back off to sleep. The next time she woke up, the sun was shining through the window above her bed and whatever Agatha had been dreaming of – if she’d even dreamt of anything – was already slipping away.

 

Agatha got dressed in Jen’s laundry room, slipping back into her clean workout clothes and tossing the clothes Jen had lent her into the washer with a few things from her hamper. She put in the detergent and fabric softener and started the load before padding back out into the kitchen, where Jen was enjoying a stupidly expensive cup of gourmet coffee.

There was another cup on the kitchen island with Agatha’s name on it, literally.

“I was wondering where that went,” Agatha said, picking up the royal purple mug with her name scribbled on it in gold, swoopy lettering.

“You left it on your desk last week,” Jen said, “at the gallery. It looked like it had been there for, like, weeks . It still had coffee in it. I brought it home to wash it. Figured you’d come here at some point to find it.”

“Aww, Jenny,” Agatha cooed, pinching her cheek as she took a sip of the coffee. Shit, that’s good . Maybe expensive was better sometimes.

Jen slapped her hand away. “Don’t call me Jenny,” she huffed. “I’m not four.”

Agatha snickered as she drank her coffee. Jen had put oatmilk and sugar in it for her, knowing just how she liked her coffee. Agatha knew Jen’s Starbucks order, too, so it really wasn’t that deep, but it still softened Agatha a little bit.

She’d never been great at making friends.

When she was little and still living with her mother, she had been the weird girl who wore clothes that didn’t quite fit her right because her mother had fished them out of their church’s lost in found bin in place of actually buying her new clothes. It wasn’t like they couldn’t have afforded new clothes. Evanora had a decent-paying job and was able to dress herself just fine. Agatha’s father had even left them with a decent chunk of money – which Agatha hadn’t seen even a penny of until her gran carried on a long legal battle for it – so it wasn’t like she couldn’t have brought her to one of the cheaper stores or even the thrift store for some decent clothes.

No, instead Agatha was dressed in mismatched patterns, ugly sweaters, and stained jeans that were all either too big or too small for her size.

Gran had bought her clothes whenever she visited, threatening Evanora to not even dare selling them or throwing them out or she would call social services on her without blinking. 

Agatha kind of wished she’d done it, but Gran had said that doing so might have actually placed Agatha in foster care. She needed Evanora to actually willingly give up her rights to Agatha. Which she did. Eventually.

When she moved to Westview to live with Gran, Agatha was the first new kid they’d had in over a decade. Most of the people in Westview were part of families that had lived there since the early twentieth century, if not earlier. Agatha’s father had grown up there, but Agatha had been given her mother’s last name (for reasons she couldn’t quite fathom since Evanora obviously hated her from birth) so nobody really knew her despite the fact that they were familiar with her grandmother.

The kids certainly didn’t know who she was on the first day of second grade, so they mostly kept their distance. Agatha was already used to this in Salem, so she didn’t even really care. She was friendly with a few classmates as she grew up, hanging out with the art kids in high school, eating lunch in the art room as they worked together on their individual projects. She’d won a few school-sponsored art shows, got her name in the local paper, received congratulations from her teachers and her gran’s friends, but she’d always been kind of a loner.

That was another reason why Gran had insisted on therapy. Agatha had made a little more of an effort to make friends (or at least better acquaintance) with some of the kids in her classes. She’d done a good enough job that she’d been asked to Senior Prom by one of the artsy boys who later came out as gay. Agatha was openly queer throughout all of high school, but she still said yes. 

They both wore suits to Prom, with matching purple pocket squares. They had fun, but Agatha never really spoke to him after graduation.

In college, she was better at socializing. She was mostly around people with similar interests, taking art classes and hanging out with her professors the same as she’d done with her high school art teachers. She had showcases and attended assigned gallery shows with her classmates.

She even lost her virginity a month into her freshman year, after a showcase, to a girl from her color theory class. Agatha couldn’t remember her name, but she wore a lot of pastels and flowers in her hair. They screwed in the bathroom on the second floor of the art building with Agatha sitting on the sink while what’s-her-face dove between her legs with everything she had.

Agatha got a lot of action in college after that, but it never interrupted her studies, and it didn’t mean that she had much in the way of friends.

The fact that Jen Kale was her friend now after an awkward one-night stand and a year of trying to get back her hoodie (which Agatha had shoved into her closet and promptly forgot about after their one night together) was a minor miracle.

Maybe not so minor to be honest.

“You coming to the show on Saturday night?” Jen asked, breaking Agatha from her thoughts. She hadn’t even realized that she’d spaced out, her coffee cup almost completely empty at that point.

“Why wouldn’t I?” Agatha asked, furrowing her brow.

Jen shrugged. “I figured, with everything going on, you might want to sit this one out.” She shook her head. “I know, it’s stupid.”

“It’s not stupid,” Agatha sighed. “I probably should sit it out, but…I just need things to be normal right now, you know? I need normal routines. Maybe I can forget that I even have this fucking shit.” She drained the rest of the cup and placed it back on the kitchen island.

“Did you get any kind of medication yet?” Jen asked. “I don’t even know what that would look like, but I’ve seen some commercials for stuff.”

“My doctor wants to put me on something with some complicated name, I dunno,” Agatha said. “It’s an injectable. Three times a week.”

“Oof,” Jen huffed. “I hate needles.”

“Me too,” Agatha said, “but she said it’s thin and gets injected into the fat, so it shouldn’t be too bad.”

“Girl, where is there fat on your body?” Jen asked, motioning to Agatha, who was back to her tank top and running shorts. Her hoodie was sitting on a stool at the island, allowing her to show off her arms and legs, her entire athletic build.

Agatha rolled her eyes. “There’s enough,” she assured Jen, lifting up her shirt a bit to show off her hips. She grabbed some of her right hip to show Jen that there was enough fat there, at least, for an injection. “And my thighs and my arms. Maybe my ass, if I get desperate. I have some fat, Jen. More than you.”

“Didn’t ever think that phrase would make me feel bad about myself,” Jen said, “but here we are.”

Agatha snorted. “Can you wash this again and bring it to the office? I need to go home, change, and maybe paint. My fingers feel all twitchy.”

Jen nodded. “Make sure you don’t forget it next time,” she said. “You’re not growing science experiments in the gallery. It’s gonna make it smell.”

“Noted,” Agatha said, slipping her hoodie over her head and making sure she had her keys, phone, and wallet. “Later, priss.”

“Later, bitch,” Jen retorted, affectionately, as she snatched their emptied mugs and brought them to the dishwasher.

 

When Agatha returned to her own house, it was quiet.

Obviously it was quiet. Agatha lived alone. The house had four bedrooms, but only one of them had been in use since her gran’s passing. The others were mainly guest rooms or her studio, which had also been the room that Gran spent her last year on Earth in. Agatha had taken over the master bedroom when Gran got sick, at her grandmother’s insistence.

“Dear, I can’t climb those steps anymore; you might as well just take it. I’ll be okay in the downstairs bedroom.”

Agatha had still spent most of her time on the first floor of the house, though, helping the nurses with her grandmother’s treatments and watching old The Price is Right and Jeopardy reruns as she told her gran about her studies. Gran had a couple of good days, but she’d never been able to climb those stairs again, instead using her energy to bake Agatha’s favorite cookies and make lunch and dinner every once in a while, which completely exasperated some of her nurses because Gran was infamous for forgetting to take things out of the oven in her last days. 

She almost burned the house down on three separate occasions, but Agatha couldn’t exactly ban her from cooking. She just made her promise to only cook when Agatha or one of the nurses was in the kitchen with her – and to always set a timer so they knew when to take something out of the oven or off the stove.

She remembered to do that about half the time, but she never nearly burned the house down again, thankfully.

After her passing, Agatha turned that bedroom into her studio. She still felt the presence of her grandmother whenever she entered it.

And it had the best light exposure with floor-to-ceiling windows that looked out into the backyard where Agatha used to play on the swing set that still sat there, a little bit old and rusted, but still usable when Agatha was feeling up to it. Before it was Gran’s bedroom, it was her sunroom, from which she would watch Agatha play on sunny afternoons in the summer or after school.

Agatha still had the loveseat that Gran had loved sitting there, facing the windows, and the little end table she’d place her cup of tea on as she read and watched Agatha over the top edge of her book. Agatha would wave to her as she swung back and forth, going higher and higher, enjoying the way the wind swept through her hair. Gran always waved back with a bright smile.

Gran had painted the room lavender well before Agatha had been born, and she’d kept it that way, painting it herself every couple of years, until she couldn’t lift a brush. Then Agatha had repainted it with a fresh coat before she made the move, adding some floral details that she knew her grandmother would love. She’d always been really big on florals.

Agatha refused to paint over them when it came time to empty the room of her grandmother’s bed and clothes and do yet another repainting a couple years later. Instead, she painted even more flowers, looking up new ones to paint whenever she got the chance.

Some of Agatha’s art now hung on the walls, both things she’d painted and pieces she’d bought from the artists that held showcases at her gallery. She only bought the ones that spoke to her, the ones that made her warm from the inside.

There was a painting, for example, of an old woman playing dress-up with a toddler, the two of them wearing feather boas, sun hats, and high heels. Their mouths are open in laughter as they each strike a pose, the corners of their eyes crinkled in their mirth. The little girl has blonde hair and green eyes, unlike Agatha, and the old lady looks nothing like her gran, but Agatha could still see herself and her gran in the painting, could hear her grandmother’s cackling laughter as they danced around a room together, just being silly like she’d never been allowed to be with her mother.

Agatha had put in a bid for the original painting almost as soon as she’d seen it. She paid probably a bit too much for it, but she wanted the real thing rather than just one of the prints they tended to sell in their gift shops after showcases.

And she’d gotten it.

One of Agatha’s paintings hung on the wall adjacent to that painting. It was of Gran, sitting on her loveseat, reading a book with a cup of tea raised halfway to her lips. The vantage point was from outside one of the windows, where Agatha had taken a picture of Gran one day when she was a teenager. The top half of the window was propped open to see Gran more clearly, but also to let in fresh air on a warm spring day. She was gazing at Agatha over the edge of that book with the red cover, one yellowed page blowing in the wind. Gran’s bright blue eyes – eyes Agatha had certainly inherited from her and Agatha’s father – twinkled at her, her eyes crinkling with a bright smile hidden by the book. Her dark hair still had some of its brown around the edges.

It was how Agatha preferred to remember her gran.

The other pieces were a mix of paintings and photographs that Agatha had collected over the years, and not all of them were from her own gallery. While Jennifer splurged on luxury versions of most everyday items, Agatha used her wealth to buy and showcase art around her house.

Only the best pieces ended up in her studio.

In the closet in the studio sat her art supplies. When Agatha walked in that day, she made a beeline for that closet, selecting a 16x20 inch canvas and setting it up on the easel she left sitting at an angle by the windows so that it could catch the most sunlight. She settled on her stool with some gesso and a flat brush and began the task of layering it on. It was tedious, but Agatha was able to get into a rhythm pretty quickly, making sure to spread it evenly before waiting for it to dry. 

Then she just had to wait. It took an annoyingly long time most days, but Agatha wasn’t honestly that concerned today.

Today, she curled up on her gran’s old loveseat, reaching for her sketchbook and pencils that sat on the end table, turned to a new page, and began to sketch.

She’d had an idea bouncing around in her brain for weeks now, her fingers itching to put it down on paper. But she’d had a lot to do with getting their next showcase set up, signing papers and negotiating with caterers and getting prints made up for the gift shop and…it was really so much to do. Agatha wished she’d known how much owning a gallery was more about making money than just appreciating good art.

They had showcases twice a month which lasted a couple of hours at a time, but Agatha felt as though they spent so much time just setting up for them that she was almost too exhausted to stop and appreciate what the local artists made. She hadn’t really connected with a piece in a long time, which frustrated her.

It made it feel as though none of what she did was even worth it anymore.

Agatha channeled every bit of her frustration into her sketch, the figure of a woman standing ankle deep in a tide starting to emerge. Agatha probably spent too much time on the detail of the ripples around the woman’s pant legs, making it clear with her black pencils that the water was soaking into the material, before she moved on to what was beyond the woman: a sunset.

Or a sunrise.

Agatha couldn’t decide.

When the idea first popped into her head, it had been a sunrise. And the woman wasn’t wearing a suit like she was now. She was wearing a flowing skirt that danced around her ankles. And the tide wasn’t wrapping around those ankles; it was lapping at her toes, just barely kissing her feet. And the woman’s arms were raised as if she were embracing the warmth of the sun.

In Agatha’s sketch, the woman’s arms weren’t visible, but it was suggested that they were crossed in front of her chest. Her hair fell loose and wild and a little tangled around her shoulders, reading to the woman’s ass, the ends broken and split and messy.

The sun was off-center to the left of the woman’s head, small and round and ringed by thick, dark lines to make it appear brighter to the observer. Agatha considered drawing clouds in the sky, but she decided against it. She liked the cloudless look of the sketch.

Then she focused a little more on the shoreline. It was straight and went right through the woman’s neck in the background. Agatha drew lines for waves, making them a little wavy and adding some shape for the seafoam until she was satisfied. 

When she pulled the sketch away from her to get a look at the whole thing, she couldn’t help but think about what might have been missing. She noticed that her hand had smudged some of the pencil around the edges and she made to erase it, but then she thought better of it. Instead, she used the side of her hand to smudge more around the edges of the sketch, adding a little more pencil to the drawing for more darkness, going and going and going until there was a fuzzy round border around the entirety of the sketch, black and messy and a little foreboding.

But she liked it. It fit, somehow.

Agatha took a deep breath as she placed the sketchbook back on the end table and stood, casting another glance at her unused canvas (it would take a long while to be ready for her oils) and padding out of the room to wash her hands and change. She still had a lot to do, and Saturday was only two days away.

She was definitely not trying to avoid thinking about anything else.

Not at all.

 

Her doctor prescribed Glatiramer Acetate and Agatha had to drop by the special pharmacy in Eastview on the day of the showcase to pick it up.

“Don’t take two doses within forty-eight hours of each other,” Doctor Calderu had said. “But make sure you don’t skip a dose. Set reminders for yourself. It’s better if you take them in the morning.”

“What are the side effects?” Agatha asked, looking at the prescription on her phone. “Will it mess me up for the day?”

“It shouldn’t,” Dr. Calderu said. “But it’s possible that you’ll feel a little tightness in your chest and pain at the injection site. I suggest you use an ice pack immediately after injection.”

“Noted,” Agatha had huffed, rolling her eyes. Why did the side effects of medications always sound worse than the disease itself? Chest pain? Jesus.

“We’ll check in with you in a few weeks,” Dr. Calderu said. “Blood test in about four weeks, okay?”

“Sure,” Agatha replied. As if I really have a choice .

Of course, she did have a choice. Dr. Calderu said that she always had the option to refuse treatment and take her chances. MS was an unpredictable disease. It could move incredibly slowly through somebody’s body, and she’d known some of her patients to put off treatment for a few years and still be perfectly fine and mobile.

At first , she had stressed. But MS always caught up to them after a while without treatment. She heavily encouraged treatment.

So Agatha had chosen the treatment, deciding that it was better to be safe than sorry. She didn’t want to risk furthering the disease on the chance that it may be one of the more quickly moving cases of MS. She’d rather not find out in a year that she could no longer go on her runs because she’d foolishly decided to take a gamble on her health.

Still, as she looked at the injector in her hand, she was seriously doubting if being unable to use her legs was really that bad.

She hated needles. It was the reason that she didn’t have any tattoos despite having a deep love and appreciation for them. Even getting her blood drawn (which she’d now have to do a whole lot more often to check her white blood cell count) made her squirm.

Still, she sucked it up as she loaded the injector and pinched the fat on her belly, closing her eyes as she followed the instructions and pressed it firmly against her skin, then pressed the button and…

Nothing. She felt nothing. Agatha glanced down at the little window and watched as the injector pressed down on it for a full ten seconds before stopping, as the instructions had said it would, before she removed it from against her skin. A drop of fluid fell from the end of the needle and there was a tiny speck of blood at the injection site, but she felt zero pain.

A little itchiness, though. Agatha placed the injector on the counter and reached for the ice pack she’d set down to soothe the pain she had been anticipating. She placed it over the injection site and sighed in relief. The itchiness immediately went away, but Agatha pressed the ice pack to her belly for a bit longer before she set about disposing of the needle and cleaning the injector. 

That wasn’t so bad , Agatha thought to herself as she placed the injector back into the carrying case she’d been sent with it. I could definitely do that again. Easy peasy.

 

Three hours later, she was scratching at the injection site under the waistband of her pants, cursing the thick material for not allowing her to get good access to it. And it also now felt like there was some kind of ball beneath the layer of skin. It was firm, like a tumor.

Shit , Agatha thought. Is that another side effect of this medication? Spontaneous, quick-growing tumors?

She’d have to remember to ask Dr. Calderu next time they spoke. Or she could Google it.

Dr. Calderu did not support the “Google It” method of learning about MS though.

Nonetheless, Agatha found herself reaching for her phone as she walked around the gallery, glancing around at the photographs on the walls. They were from some Advanced Photography class offered by the Westview Community Center. The instructor had a good relationship with Jen Kale and had her own photographs featured in a decent number of their previous showcases, so when she requested that her students be given the same opportunity – on her dime, of course – Jen had jumped at the chance.

“Sharon’s art has always been amazing ,” Jen had said, as if trying to convince Agatha, who was already on board. “It stands to reason that her protegés would be just as good.”

“As long as she pays for everything,” Agatha said, “I’m cool with it.”

Sharon Hart had paid for everything, from the catering to the prints that would be sold in the gift shops. Agatha just had to order and organize everything, but the photographer had basically given her a blank check. That is how much she believed in her students’ work.

It was admirable, Agatha thought.

And she wasn’t wrong, actually. Her students’ photographs were actually incredible . Agatha forgot what she was googling the second she glanced up at a photograph of a shadowy figure with a cane standing on a stage. There was a spotlight behind the figure, the flare of light casting their shape in a glow, but their hair was wispy and the shape and tilt of their head suggested a smile as they cocked their hip, one leg crossed over the other. Agatha couldn’t see a single feature of that face, but they were breathtaking, nonetheless, oozing confidence in their stance.

The name on the label next to the photograph said Dulce . No last name. Just Dulce.

Sweet , Agatha’s brain translated for her. Sweet photography. She didn’t actually know if that was the name of the photo or the name of the artist. Either way, it kinda fit.

“Do you like it?” a shy voice said behind her. Agatha turned around to see a young woman standing there in a black dress and a gray sweater, a cropped haircut that came to just below her ears with a blue streak on the fringe over her left eye, and a stud in her button nose. 

Agatha offered her a gentle smile. “I love it,” she said. “It’s clear, has good exposure, and the subject is interesting. Well done.”

“Thanks,” the young woman said. She had to be a teenager, Agatha thought. A college student at the oldest. “I’m Dulce.” The girl held out her hand.

“Dulce,” Agatha echoed. “Nice to meet you. Agatha Harkness. I’m one of the owners of the gallery.”

“I know,” Dulce said, immediately flushing at Agatha’s raised brows. She shrugged. “My sister and I have been here before for other showcases. Usually photography, but we’ve also seen some of your art. And your partner’s. You’re a really talented painter.”

“And you are a really talented photographer,” Agatha said, motioning to the photo. “Keep up the good work and you’ll go far. Maybe you’ll even end up with your work on exhibition at MoMA.”

Dulce’s eyes widened at that. She had big, expressive eyes. She was so young.

“You really think so?” she asked, glancing back at her photograph. 

Agatha nodded, also looking at the photo. “Your use of the light and dark is impressive,” she said. “At first glance, it looks like a black and white photo, but there are slight traces of color there, like the brown of the stage and the yellow of the spotlight behind your subject. Who is that, by the way?”

“My sister,” Dulce said, smiling affectionately. “She’s almost always the subject of my photos. She says it’s annoying, but I know she secretly loves it.” She laughed.

“And where is she tonight?” Agatha asked.

“She’s busy on Saturdays,” Dulce informed her, “but that’s fine. She has her own art to focus on, and she said that she’ll be here in spirit, anyway.” She rolled her eyes and motioned to the photo of her sister’s figure.

Cheesy , Agatha thought in amusement.

“Well, she looks great,” Agatha said, “even if I can’t really see her face. I hope she’ll be your subject for many more years to come.”

“Me too,” Dulce said. “Thanks for your kind words, Miss Harkness.”

“Agatha,” she corrected. “Please. It was nice meeting you, Dulce.”

“You too,” Dulce said, beaming up at her. She was so small, too.

She remembered being that small, but never that hopeful.

Agatha brushed a hand over Dulce’s shoulder as she made her way to the next piece. The photographs were all really good, but none of them invoked the same awe in Agatha as Dulce’s. Maybe it was the fact that the figure had a cane and it was affecting Agatha because of her very recent diagnosis. Who knew?

Agatha placed a bid on the original, making it big enough that nobody would likely challenge her on it.

“Have you finally found a photograph that moves you, Agatha Harkness?” Jen teased as Agatha finished writing down her bid for Dulce’s Untitled . “I never thought I’d see the day.” She handed Agatha a champagne flute.

“I have photographs in my house,” Agatha huffed.

“ Prints ,” Jen reminded her. “Never the original. Originals are the real deal. That must have made quite the impression. Which one is it?”

“You’ll find out,” Agatha said, sipping on the bubbly champagne, “if I win the bid.”

“You will,” Jen huffed. “You always win the bids because you always bid way more money than these pieces are worth.”

“Gotta support your local artists,” Agatha shot back. “Especially ones that need help paying for college.”

“College?” Jen asked, wrinkling her nose. “Sharon’s students all have masters’ degrees.”

“What?” Agatha gasped, her eyes widening. “You’re kidding. Even that one?” She motioned to where Dulce was speaking with another patron, a bright smile on her lips as she bounced on the balls of her feet.

“Oh, Dulce ,” Jen snorted. “Yeah, that one’s a prodigy, actually. At least according to Sharon. She graduated early from high school, undergrad, and grad. She’s twenty-three.”

“That’s impossible,” Agatha huffed. “Look at her. That’s a full infant.”

Jen shrugged. “Kids these days are getting younger and younger…I blame the skincare industry.” Agatha snorted, taking another sip of her drink. “How are you holding up?” Jen asked. “Do you need a rest?”

“I’m fine , Jen,” Agatha huffed. “My spine is still fully intact. I ran five miles this morning.”

“So you’re good to come out for drinks after the showcase?”

“Absolutely not.”

“Ugh, you’re such a grandma,” Jen grumbled.

“Considering my grandma was cool as fuck,” Agatha retorted, “I’ll take that as a compliment.”

Jen rolled her eyes. “Whatever,” she said. “Old lady. Thinking twenty-three year olds are babies.”

“Listen, that child definitely has Benjamin Button disease, okay? Or she’s lying about who she is.”

“And you ,” Jen said, “are supporting her.” Agatha’s eyes widened in surprise. “Please,” Jen snorted. “You wouldn’t be so invested in this girl’s age if you weren’t wowed by her artwork. You’re going all Mama Bear again.”

“ Again ?” Agatha gasped. “When was the first time?”

“Billy Kaplan,” Jen said immediately. “The little twink from Eastview who worships the ground you walk on.”

“Don’t call him a twink,” Agatha said, her voice taking on a warning tone.

“Exhibit A,” Jen said, motioning to Agatha, who just rolled her eyes in response and took another sip of her champagne. “It’s cool to be a good mentor to these kids. Goddess knows we could have used that when we were just starting out. It’s nice that you get invested with the younger artists. You should really think about having kids, you know?”

“I’m twenty-nine.”

“And? My mom was twenty-six when she had me,” Jen pointed out, “and that was after waiting to establish herself in her career before she even started trying. You are established. You have the money. Have the child.”

“Don’t pressure me,” Agatha huffed. “Why don’t you have a child?”

“Bitch, I might,” Jen said. “Eventually. I’m only twenty-nine.”

“I am going to hit you,” Agatha said, frowning at her.

“No, you won’t,” Jen said, booping her nose. She practically sprinted away when Agatha made an honest-to-God swing at her, cackling and blowing a kiss over her shoulder.

Agatha couldn’t suppress her grin as she watched her go.

 

When she got home later that night, she had her new art piece under her arm. Agatha made her way straight to the studio, flipping on the lights and placing it up against the wall, trying to decide where to put it. She didn’t have the energy to find another command hook just yet, but she did pick a spot next to her own painting for it, right in the corner of the room. It would look good there, she decided. She made a plan to hang it up the next day, after her run.

Before she shut off the lights, Agatha took another good look at the figure in the photograph: Dulce’s sister. She wondered if she looked like Dulce, if she were younger or older, and how she might react – how they might both react – to know that this photograph was hanging in Agatha’s personal studio, where only her favorite art pieces went.

“I think they’d be ecstatic, peanut,” she could imagine her gran saying. “You are exceptional, after all. Anybody would be lucky to have their art piece hanging up in here.”

Exceptional . That’s what Gran had always told her. She’d repeated it again and again until Agatha somewhat believed it. And then more and more until Agatha actually believed it.

She was exceptional, as were all the other artists whose work hung on these walls.

Agatha smiled to herself as she finally shut off the light and closed her studio door, making her way upstairs to her bedroom. She changed out of the suit she’d worn for the showcase and donned an old shirt with her college’s name on it, frowning as her knuckles brushed the hard lump beneath her skin. It was a little sensitive, but it didn’t irk her as much as it had earlier.

She just hoped it wasn’t anything serious.

Chapter End Notes

If you wanna picture Dulce, picture Jenna Ortega. That's who plays her in my brain.

Comments are always welcome.

Chapter 3: Community

Chapter Summary

Agatha's doctor encourages her to try Just One Meeting with the MS support group and she finally gives in, organizing an escape plan. The women she meets there, however...well they may be more interesting than she'd originally thought.

One, in particular...

Chapter Notes

If you see anything spelt wrong, no you don't. I have a degree in creative writing but I'm still only mostly human so cut me a break.

Also, I will admit right now that I have never been to an MS support group. I hope that my depiction is accurate for anybody who HAS been to a support group of some kind.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Chapter Three

“It’s normal,” Dr. Calderu assured her that Monday when Agatha called. “Sometimes it just happens. Putting something warm over the injection site immediately after the injection should help, as should massaging the spot a little bit. I can’t guarantee that it will always work, but roughly four times out of five, it does.”

“And there’s no other medication I can possibly take that doesn’t involve sticking a needle into my body three times a week?” Agatha asked, trying not to sound like she was whining. She’d just done her second injection and it still didn’t hurt when she stuck the needle in, but there was another bump under her skin that was very sensitive to touch, right at the waistband of her running shorts. 

Luckily, she’d already gone for her run that morning, but she didn’t want to deal with it chafing over the next few days because that would get real old real fast. 

“There are,” Lilia said, “but I don’t suggest them. They wreak havoc on your digestive system and cause your hair to thin.”

Agatha looked in the mirror at her bushy dark brown hair. She had just taken it out of the braid she’d put it in for her run. When she was a little girl, her mother had complained endlessly about that hair. She’d run brushes through it roughly, never bothering to make sure that she wasn’t hurting her daughter in the process ‘fixing’ her hair. Agatha had cried too many times because of Evanora’s roughness, had been slapped in the mouth for it, and had learned to hold her lips shut tightly.

Once, when she was six, Evanora had brought her to the hair salon for a “trim” and had instructed the stylist to cut off over a foot of Agatha’s long, curly, unruly (according to Evanora) hair so that it fell to just below her ears. Agatha had sobbed uncontrollably and nearly got herself stabbed by the scissors several times in the process. The hairstylist had shed tears, apologizing profusely as she worked, rubbing Agatha’s back as she snipped off the hair. Evanora had wanted her to use buzzers, but she’d negotiated the bob, cleaning it up as much as she could to make it look as nice as possible. Agatha’s throat was raw as she was forced to thank the stylist, who just nodded tearfully in response.

By the time her hair had grown back to its original glory, Gran had taken her in.

Agatha ran her fingers through her hair now. As bushy as it could get, she took good care of it with countless products and regular trims to cut away the split ends. She had straightened it for a while in high school and college, but her sophomore year girlfriend had seen it drying naturally after a shower and had told Agatha that it looked so good curly and that she just needed to learn how to style it.

So Agatha did learn. She bought new products to put in it during and after showers, had learned how to brush it out while using a hairdryer, and had even learned how to brush it from the bottom to reduce pain from hitting knots. She thought about the little girl who had cried silent tears while getting her hair brushed, who had screamed while it had nearly all been chopped off. 

She had hated her hair when she was little. It was another thing that other kids made fun of. But it had been hers and her mother had forcibly cut it and that was more upsetting than the fact that she’d had to have short hair for so many months afterward.

Agatha clutched two big chunks of her hair and held them up above her head. It was thick as ever, sometimes poking up at odd places when she woke up from a particularly deep sleep. Jen had given her a bonnet to wear to bed, which Agatha used from time to time, but she often either tied it in braids or let it stay wild and free.

Agatha loved her hair now. If it ‘thinned’ she didn’t know what that would really mean for her. Would it lose its curl? It would definitely lose its volume. Would it fall flat? Would it feel like straw? Would it come out in clumps? Would she wake up surrounded by piles of her own dark hair, leaving her completely bald?

“Agatha?” Dr. Calderu’s voice broke her from her thoughts and she turned toward the bathroom counter where she’d left her phone on speaker. “Did we get disconnected?”

“No,” Agatha said, quickly. “Sorry. I just…I was thinking. Is there anything else that I could take that’s not three times a week?”

“There are many treatment options,” Dr. Calderu confirmed, “but let’s stay on this course for now. Once we get to your three-month check in, we’ll see if it’s working. Have you had any other side effects? Chest pain?”

“No,” Agatha sighed, her shoulders slumping as she looked at herself in the mirror. “No chest pain. Just the annoying lumps.”

“Hot compress,” Dr. Calderu reiterated. “It should really help. Otherwise, just let me know if there’s anything else of concern, like numbness in your extremities or dizziness.”

“Dizziness?” Agatha asked.

“Well, it’s a neurological disease,” Dr. Calderu reminded her. “It’s not super common at your stage, but some patients have reported vertigo.”

“I don’t get dizzy,” Agatha said. “I think I’m physically incapable, honestly.”

“I’ve never heard that before,” Dr. Calderu chuckled. “By the way, have you checked out the support group I told you about?”

“Um,” Agatha said, “not yet. I…haven’t really had the time.” She cleared her throat. “I had a showcase this weekend and we’re getting some new pieces of the gallery this week and I still have my own art to do…you know, I’m just busy.”

“Dear,” Dr. Calderu said, sounding way too much like Agatha’s gran, “you need a support system. You need people around you, especially at this time when everything is so fresh and new and scary.”

“It’s not that scary,” Agatha huffed. It was a total lie. It was terrifying . 

But Dr. Calderu didn’t need to know that. 

“Still,” Dr. Calderu said. “Just go once. I’ve been there before. Most of the ladies there are my patients and they are a hoot , I’ll tell you that!” She cackled. “Just one time and if you don’t like it, I’ll leave you alone, okay?”

Agatha groaned, leaning over the sink, pressing her forehead to the cool metal of the faucet. “Fine,” she said. “One time. When does it meet again?”

“Mondays and Thursdays,” Dr. Calderu informed her. “Do you still have the pamphlet I gave you?”

“Yes,” Agatha said, walking out of the bathroom. The pamphlet sat, untouched, on her dresser where she’d left it the day she ran all the way around Westview and collapsed, sobbing, into Jen Kale’s arms. 

“It has all the information you need. I believe they meet at six PM both nights. They really are amazing people.”

“I’m sure,” Agatha said, padding over to the dresser to pluck the glossy paper between her fingers. “I just…I dunno. I feel a little weird about it, you know?”

“Understandable,” Dr. Calderu said in a voice that was way too soft for Agatha to handle right now. “You wouldn’t be the first of my patients to feel this way, but the ones who had taken my advice and gone to the support group typically found that it helped, in some small way, to make them feel as though they weren’t so alone. Most of them don’t even attend every meeting.”

“Then what’s even the point?” Agatha huffed, dropping down onto her bed, looking up at her ceiling fan.

“They go when they want to be around others who understand what they, themselves, are going through,” Dr. Calderu said gently. “Sometimes, it just takes one meeting to make you realize that you are not alone in this world, that there are others who share your very same burden.”

“Thanks, Gandhi,” Agatha huffed.

Dr. Calderu snorted. “Please,” she said, “just attend one meeting. If you don’t like it, then I will stop bothering you about it and leave you to rot alone in your apartment.”

“Promise?” Agatha asked.

“Promise,” Dr. Calderu said. “Now, if you have no more questions, I have an appointment that I must prepare for. Do not hesitate to call me if there are any further issues with your treatment.”

“Okay,” Agatha sighed. “Thanks.”

“Anytime.” 

The line went dead and Agatha sighed, rolling onto her side with her phone still in her hand. She glanced at the clock.

It was 12:37 PM on a Monday. There was a meeting in about five hours. Part of her wanted to skip it and just wait until Thursday. The other part of her was bored with nothing else to do today (they didn’t have a showcase for at least ten days and pretty much everything had been handled by the people who had booked the space so there wasn’t much for her and Jen to do, anyway) and just wanted to get this over with.

Agatha breathed a deep, dramatic sigh and dialed Jen’s number.

She answered on the third ring: “What’s up?”

“What are you doing tonight?” Agatha asked, rolling back to look at the ceiling.

“Smoking meth,” Jen retorted. “It’s Monday night, what do you think I’m doing?”

“Can you drive me somewhere?”

“Depends on where and when,” Jen hummed, “I’m not staying out all night with you.”

“All night?” Agatha scoffed. “It’s like you don’t even know me. No, don’t worry, you’ll be all tucked in between your absurdly expensive satin sheets before eight.”

“Those sheets were worth every penny, I’ll have you know,” Jen retorted. “Anyway, where are we going?”

“To the Westview Community Center.”

“Eww,” Jen said, “why?”

“What’s wrong with it?” Agatha asked, furrowing her brow. 

“It smells like feet.”

“How do you know that?”

“I signed up for a jewelry-making course there and promptly dropped out when I smelled the feet. It cost me fifty bucks.”

“You are weak,” Agatha snorted.

“Aren’t you the one that needs a ride?” Jen asked. “Are you sure you want to insult me?”

“To be fair, I don’t need the ride because I can’t walk or drive myself; I need the ride for emotional support.”

“That only makes your insults worse.”

“I’m so very sorry, Jennifer Kale,” Agatha said, putting on a sickly-sweet tone. “Can you find it in your heart to forgive me and do me this favor?”

“Hmm…” Jen hummed, dragging it on for way too long. “Fine,” she said, at last. “But only because I have nothing better to do until Real Housewives is on.”

Agatha bit back her comment about Jen still enjoying trashy reality TV. She knew that would get her absolutely nowhere and might even cause Jen to dissolve their entire partnership. 

Nobody messed with Jen’s Real Housewives .

“Thank you, Jen,” Agatha said with a sigh. “I promise I won’t make you go in and smell feet.”

“Damn right you won’t,” Jen huffed. “But, seriously, why the community center of all places?”

“Um, I’ll explain later,” Agatha said, “on the way. I seriously need a shower right now.” She sniffed her armpit and recoiled. “Pick me up at a quarter to six.”

“Yup,” Jen said. “You’re buying me dinner.”

“Deal.”

 

Agatha didn’t know what one was expected to wear to an MS support group. She had half a mind to call Dr. Calderu and ask her, but that really wasn’t the woman’s job. She definitely had other more important things to do than give Agatha fashion advice.

After her shower, Agatha still had at least three hours to get ready. She spent a good hour of that time playing with her hair in the mirror. She used a towel to pat it dry first, then reached for the blow dryer to style it into something a little more manageable than her wild curly look (but not by much) and stuck it into a bun while she tried to figure out how she was going to style it.

She had an endless amount of hair products (thanks in large part to Jen, who insisted that they were all necessary to take care of her “3b curly hair pattern” whatever that meant) but wasn’t even sure how to use the majority of them tonight. She knew that she didn’t want to keep her hair in its half-assed ponytail, if only because the elastic she’d used to put it up looked ready to snap at any second. 

She made a note to get more scrunchies at the store (where the hell did they all go?) and tried to focus more on what she was going to wear. 

Her closet was a mishmash of sweaters, slacks, mom jeans, skinny jeans, flowy blouses, t-shirts, tank tops, running shorts, sports bras, granny panties (they were more comfortable), nice lingerie (which was gathering dust, unfortunately), a few dresses (mainly for events) and a fitted suit or two (or five) that she’d splurged on for herself. There were also some pajamas and a couple of robes (one of which she was wearing because she didn’t like putting on clothes immediately after a shower, usually), but otherwise all that was left were her shoes. 

She had nothing to wear though.

Agatha groaned, padding back to her bed and throwing herself down on it, quite dramatically. She reached for her phone and did a quick Google search.

Comfortable and casual attire is generally appropriate if no other dress code has been discussed prior to the meeting.

Agatha wondered what they considered ‘comfortable and casual’ attire. Her workout clothes were both comfortable and casual, but they didn’t feel appropriate to wear to a place where everybody would probably be sitting in a kumbaya circle and talking about their feelings.

Agatha wrinkled her nose; she hated talking about her feelings. It was the reason why she had quit therapy as a teen – then again as an adult after her gran died. She didn’t need to talk to anybody else about how she felt about how shitty her life had been and how shitty it was again now that she had this dumb diagnosis. And she really didn’t want to listen to others talk about how shitty their lives were living with it.

But Dr. Calderu didn’t seem the type to let something like this go. She seemed like the kind of woman who might spend the next few months (years?) gently prodding at Agatha to attend a meeting until she finally acquiesced.

Agatha groaned again and pushed herself up into a sitting position, glaring at her closet across the bedroom.

Just one night. Just one outfit. She could do this. She could spend an hour listening to some women complain about their stupid autoimmune disease and how it affected their lives. She could force a smile and pretend that she’s listening even as she just thought about all the things she needed to do this week, like picking up her dry cleaning and going to the grocery store for more oatmilk and bread. If it got to be too much, she could text Jen to call her with an ‘emergency’ and leave early.

She could do this.

With renewed purpose, Agatha stood and made her way back to the closet.

 

“An MS support group,” Jen echoed as she took off from the curb in front of Agatha’s house, “really?” Her brows reached what might have been her hairline if she’d had hair. “Wow, good for you, Agatha.”

“Fuck off,” Agatha huffed, crossing her arms over her chest. “My doctor is making me go.”

“Is the doctor in the car with us right now?” Jen snorted, glancing in the rearview mirrors. “Does she have a sniper trained on the car? I just got this thing detailed, Agatha.”

Agatha rolled her eyes. “Oh, haha,” she said, “you’re a comedian. No, she just won’t stop bothering me until I try it out. She says that I need a ‘support system’ or whatever because my entire family is dead.”

“I mean…” Jen said. “That wouldn’t be such a terrible idea.” Agatha shot her a look of betrayal. “Look, bitch,” Jen sighed. “I love you. You are somehow one of my dearest friends despite ghosting me in college–”

“How many times do I have to apologize for that?”
