Jen went on as if Agatha hadn’t spoken: “And I’ll always be here to talk and watch shitty romcoms with you and support you through this whole shitty situation…but you do need a community. I’m just one singular person, and, as far as I know, I do not have any neurological autoimmune diseases, so I can’t really relate to the whole MS thing. But these women can , right? It’s, like, specifically women in this group?” She waited.

“Yeah,” Agatha answered after a moment. “Women with MS, specifically.”

“Okay then,” Jen said with a decisive nod. “These are women who can relate with you, to you. And you can relate to them. It’s not going to hurt you to try a little bit. Hell, most support groups don’t even make you talk. You can just listen.”

“How do you know that?” Agatha asked, slumping a little more in her seat as she saw the community center coming up on the right. 

“I got detention one too many times in middle school,” Jen admitted. “My parents were concerned that I was ‘going down a bad path’ so they decided to put me in a support group of similarly troubled youths.” She rolled her eyes. “None of us were really troubled; we just didn’t like some classes and we all had smart mouths. They took me out when I promised to do better at school.”

“Did you?” Agatha asked, half-grinning even as they pulled into the community center parking lot.

“Eh, I did alright,” Jen said, noncommittally. “But they never made me speak at group is the point. They tend to just wait you out, at least at that group.”

“I feel like my doctor has spies there, though,” Agatha said. “Like somebody is going to report to her whether or not I actually participated and then I’ll somehow be in trouble if I don’t.”

“What?” Jen snorted. “Like you’re going to get a bad grade in group therapy?”

“I dunno!” Agatha huffed, throwing her hands up in the air. “Maybe! If I don’t give it a real shot, she’ll just keep bugging me until I do, you know?”

“Then give it a real shot,” Jen suggested, parking the car right in view of the doors. “If anybody can understand what you’re going through, they can, right?” 

“Thank you, Aristotle,” Agatha huffed, undoing her seatbelt. “If I text you SOS, please call me and pretend there’s an emergency that needs my attention so I can leave.”

“Only if you text me after the thirty minute mark,” Jen said, reaching into the glove compartment for a book. “Until then, this is my ‘me time’.”

Agatha rolled her eyes. “Whatever,” she said, slipping out of the car. 

Jen was already absorbed into whatever trashy romance novel she was reading.

 

Agatha didn’t know exactly what feet smelled like, but this was probably close to whatever the scent was, she guessed. 

The main room of the community center was really just a basketball court with a stage on one end of the room. There were folded up bleachers on the left and right walls and a circle of about a dozen chairs right in the center of the court. Not far away from this circle of chairs was a table with different refreshments spread on them, including homemade cupcakes, Oreos, and a Box o’ Joe from Dunkin. Agatha grabbed a styrofoam cup and filled it with coffee, her eyes widening when she saw that one of the milk options they had out was oat.

“Nice,” she murmured, pouring some of the milk into her coffee and stirring it with a wooden stick. Then she grabbed a couple of Oreos and turned toward the circle of metal folding chairs.

There were a few people sitting down already, talking amongst themselves. 

A woman with hair that went from strawberry blonde to pale yellow at the roots sat with another woman whose hair was short, choppy, and almost platinum blonde. They were speaking animatedly in what Agatha assumed was Russian, though she had never actually studied the language.

Another pair of women sat to Agatha’s right. They both had brown hair, but one was obviously older than the other. The younger of the two had her hair in a long ponytail and spoke animatedly with her hands. She had on a tight black sweater and dark ripped jeans. The older woman wore a red blouse and a navy blue skirt, her feet encased in black ballet flats. Her hair was pinned up on her head like she was a fifties centerfold. Her brown eyes had wrinkles at the corners when she laughed, which she seemed to do often.

Directly across from where Agatha decided to sit sat two more women, both around her age, by Agatha’s estimate. The shorter of the two had choppy black hair with orange-red highlights throughout. She wore a red leather jacket and tight black jeans. She had three piercings in each of her ears and one on her eyebrow. She looked oddly familiar…

The taller woman had black hair, as well, but there were no other colors dyed in, and most of it was hidden by a forest green beanie that slouched on her head. She did have piercings, however. There was a stud in her left nostril and several hoops lining the shells of her ears. When she smiled, Agatha could also see a tongue piercing that made something flutter low in her stomach. She wondered if she had any other piercings…

As if feeling Agatha’s gaze on her, the other woman glanced up and gave her a lopsided smirk, showing off a tiny gap between her two front teeth. She straightened up in her chair, the giant holes ripped into the knees of her jeans stretching a bit as more of her flesh poked through them. Her skin was olive-toned and smooth. Agatha felt the urge to reach out and touch it, to feel its softness.

But that would have been dumb, if not only because the woman was about six or seven feet away from her.

To distract herself from those thoughts (and those piercing gold-flecked brown eyes) Agatha shoved an Oreo into her mouth and chased it with a large gulp of her coffee, turning her gaze away to look down at her shoes. She’d chosen her Converse. The dark purple color matched the sweater she’d plucked out of her sweater, a white collared shirt beneath it. She also wore jeans, but hers were a lighter wash than the woman’s across from her and had zero tears in them. She looked like she was working in a library or something, but most of the women had on similar clothing – more or less.

The woman across from her wore a t-shirt with a picture of a suggestively-shaped cactus sticking out of a pot. Beneath it, in bold writing, were the words “Don’t be a Prick!” that Agatha couldn’t help but snort at. The second the sound left her, all eyes turned in her direction and she felt her face heat up as the hot woman’s (because there was no way to deny that she was hot) gaze met hers again, her head tilting a bit in curiosity. 

Agatha shoved another Oreo into her mouth and gulped down some more coffee, only wincing slightly at the burn in her throat. She was going to feel that on her tongue for the next few days, she just knew it.

She avoided the woman’s gaze by checking her nails, which hadn’t seen a salon in far too long. Maybe, she thought, she should make an appointment soon. She was due a little pampering. Jen would probably insist on coming, too.

“Good evening, ladies!” a voice said from the direction of the door. Agatha turned her head to see a woman with bright green eyes, fiery red hair, and a pair of crutches attached to her arms as she walked across the floor to a chair on Agatha’s right.

Despite the crutches, the woman lowered herself quite gracefully down into the chair, like she was somehow lighter than air or something. Agatha was both impressed and jealous.

She also wondered if that was how her future looked (the crutches, not the floating on air). 

“How’s everyone doing tonight?” the woman asked, smiling at the women sitting around the circle. “I see a lot of my usuals, but…” she stopped at Agatha, her smile widening, “also a newcomer! Welcome! So happy to have you here!”

Agatha gave an awkward wave, but made no move to stand up. She shoved her last Oreo in her mouth and drained the last of her coffee instead.

“Well, since we have a newcomer,” the woman said, chipperly, “it’s once again time to make introductions.” She cleared her throat and placed a hand on her chest. “My name is Wanda Maximoff. I am forty years old. I was diagnosed two years ago with Primary Progressive MS, but my doctors think that I’ve had it for at least a little while longer. I got these bad boys,” she patted the crutches, “about a year ago and I’ve been doing okay with them so far. My husband and my boys help a lot, too. My twins are twelve and a handful, but I love them. They come with me to meetings, sometimes, for support.” She shrugged. “What else…oh! I’m from Sokovia, originally, but I moved here when I was a kid, so my accent is all but gone. But it really comes out when I get excited about something, just so you know.” 

She was looking specifically at Agatha as she said this. Agatha nodded.

“Okay,” Wanda said, clapping her hands on her thighs. “Well, why don’t we quickly go around the circle for more introductions? Let’s start with Peggy here.” She turned to the older brunette woman who was sitting in the seat right next to her.

Peggy nodded, standing up with a soft grunt.

“Hello,” she said, with a posh British accent, “my name is Peggy Carter. I am fifty-two years old and I was in the British Airforce for well over a decade before retiring and settling down with my spouse. I was diagnosed with RRMS about five years ago, and it’s been a rather slow progression. I’m doing well, I think. I can walk almost as well as I used to, but sometimes I still find myself taking one of those scooters at the grocery store because I just can’t be arsed to walk around.”

“Amen!” one of the blonde women said, eliciting laughter from around the room.

“Thank you, Peggy,” Wanda said. Peggy nodded and sat down, primly smoothing her skirt down beneath herself. “Maria?”

The second brunette stood a little bit unevenly, but she had a strong, athletic build. 

“Hey, y’all,” she said. “You already know me. My name is Maria Hill. I am thirty-nine years old. I was also in the airforce, but I was stateside. Obviously, I am not British. I was born in Canada though, so that’s a fun fact about me, I guess. I’ve only had my diagnosis for a year, but I’ve doing fine. The injections suck, though.”

“They really do,” the Asian woman sitting across from Agatha said, with more murmurs of agreement. Agatha just nodded.

“Thank you,” Wanda said with a laugh as Maria sat back down. “Rio?”

The woman that Agatha had been locking eyes with every so often nodded and stood, waying a little bit, though she tried to act casual about it.

“Hey,” she said, addressing Agatha directly, “my name is Rio Vidal. I am thirty-five years old, practically the baby of this group, and I got diagnosed two years ago, after I took a bad fall on stage.” At Agatha’s raised eyebrows, Rio went on, shrugging one shoulder. “I’m a stand-up comedian,” she explained. “Or, well, actually a sit-down comedian, I guess.” She chuckled at her own joke, scratching the back of her neck. “I perform weekly at the Witches’ Road on Friday and Saturday nights.” 

The way she said it made it sound like an invitation. Agatha made a mental note to check it out. She’d heard of the bar. It was on the other side of Westview from where she lived, but she’d never gone there. Maybe Jen knew of it. Maybe Jen would want to go see a comedy show…

“We all make a point to go at least once a month,” Wanda informed Agatha. “To support our funny girl.”

Rio flushed slightly and rolled her eyes, sitting back down. The woman next to her laughed and patted her shoulder. Rio shrugged her off, but she was grinning.

And still looking at Agatha.

The next woman stood without Wanda’s prompting.

“My name is Alice Wu-Gulliver,” she said. “I’m thirty-eight, and I was diagnosed four years ago with RRMS. I’ve been okay for the most part, except I have this tremor in my dominant hand.” She held up her right hand, which did tremor noticeably. “I know how to play pretty much all major instruments – piano, guitar, bass, violin, etc – but I really only play drums right now. I take medication for the tremors, but they’re too unpredictable to really play anything that needs finer motor skills. I do pretty well on my drum solos still.” She pulled a pair of drumsticks from her back pocket and air-drummed a solo in front of her. A few around the circle chuckled at that and Alice beamed before sitting down.

Next was the woman with the choppy platinum hair.

“Hello,” she said in a thick Russian accent, “my name is Yelena Belova and I do not have MS. I am purely my sister’s emotional support animal.”

“Stop calling yourself an animal, freak,” the other woman said, her voice clear and very American. She tugged Yelena down and used her shoulder to shove herself to her feet.

“If you would just get me a dog –”

“You’re a grown woman,” the ginger-blonde woman said. “Get yourself a dog.” She offered Agatha an amused smirk. “My name is Natasha Romanoff,” she said. “I am forty-one. This is my dumb little sister, who is only here because the numbness in my right foot has left me unable to really drive.”

“Also because I am very supportive,” Yelena pointed out. “And Natasha always buys me a milkshake afterward.”

Natasha rolled her eyes, smirking at Agatha. “I can practically hear you wondering why only one of us has an accent,” she said.

Agatha was, but she didn’t confirm nor deny it. She just stared. 

“Well, one of us took speech therapy seriously as a child,” Natasha said. “We came here when I was thirteen and Yelena was seven. I immediately got into speech therapy and Yelena outright refused to even learn English for several years .” She kicked Yelena’s boot. “Stubborn ass,” she huffed.

“You see how she abuses me?” Yelena huffed, crossing her arms. “Rude.”

Natasha rolled her eyes. “Before I lost the use of my foot, I was a private eye,” she said. “Yelena worked with me and took over most of the footwork. I still do a lot of the behind-the-scenes stuff, but if you see me in public and I don’t immediately say hi to you? No, you didn’t.”

Agatha nodded as others chuckled.

“Thank you, Nat,” Wanda said, warmly. Then she turned to Agatha, expectantly.

Agatha cleared her throat and stood up, sticking her thumbs into the pockets of her jeans. “Hi,” she said, clearing her throat, “my name is Agatha Harkness. I was diagnosed a little less than a week ago.” She saw some sympathy and understanding flash across faces around the circle and she hated it. “My doctor said that my diagnosis is pretty early, though. Like unheard of early. And it was because my stupid hand went fuzzy, like, a year ago.” She shook her head, flexing her left hand. “I never thought this would happen to me, honestly. I’m a runner, and I paint, and I take care of myself – for the most part. I mean, I just shoved three full Oreos into my mouth, which isn’t super uncommon for me, now that I think of it.” She laughed, nervously. Others laughed with her. Agatha cleared her throat. “I also own The Craft in Westview.”

“The art gallery?” Rio asked, her eyes sparking with recognition.

Agatha felt her face warm up. “Yes,” she said. “My partner and I opened it right after grad school. Oh, I’m twenty-nine, by the way.”

“A new baby!” Maria said, clapping her hands. “Rio isn’t the youngest anymore!”

“Happy day,” Rio deadpanned, rolling her eyes, but she grinned again, sticking her tongue into her cheek. 

Agatha felt butterflies in her stomach for some dumb reason. “Well, anyway,” Agatha said, looking down at her feet. “If you like art, we have showcases twice a month, and the gallery is open most days for casual perusal. We have a gift shop, too, where we sell the prints, so…come by if you want.”

“Are you there a lot?” Rio asked before Agatha could sit down.

“Most days,” she said, her heart pounding a bit at the way she had Rio’s undivided attention. “I usually get there around noon and leave at four or five. We’re closed on Sundays and Mondays, though.”

Why the hell was she giving this intense stranger her work schedule?

“Good to know,” Rio said, nodding. “Maybe I’ll come by.”

“Good,” Agatha said, blushing. Good? GOOD?!?!

She dropped heavily back into her seat and checked the time on her phone. She still had at least fifteen minutes before she was allowed to call Jen for a rescue.

Damn her and those stupid trashy novels.

Agatha avoided Rio’s gaze, focusing instead on Wanda, who was still smiling way too brightly for her liking. “Welcome, Agatha!” she said. “It’s so nice to have you here with us. We hope you come back for more meetings.”

“Thanks,” Agatha said, but she wasn’t planning on it. She was counting down the seconds until she could text her SOS to Jen.

Fourteen minutes left.

“So,” Wanda said, clapping her hands, “does anybody have anything new to share about their lives? Any life updates or major events?” She looked around the circle.

Alice raised her hand. “I’m going on tour with my mom for a few weeks,” she said. “So I probably won’t be around much.”

“Well, that’s exciting!” Wanda said. “Where are you going?”

“Just around New England,” Alice said. “Her drummer had some family emergency, so she was kind of in a bind. He should be back by the time they leave for the European leg of the tour.”

“Still!” Wanda said. “You’re going to be touring with The Coral Shore , a major rock band!”

“The Coral…is your mom Lorna Wu ?” Agatha gasped.

“One and the same,” Alice confirmed, flushing slightly. “Yeah. I’ve been going on tours with her since I was a kid, but I was raised mostly by my dad. Most people barely know I exist. Mainly because my mom was totally insistent that I get a normal upbringing and not be thrust into a spotlight that was not of my own choosing.” She rolled her eyes, but affectionately. “She started letting me perform with her almost as soon as I was eighteen, so long as I kept my grades up at university and whatnot.”

“Wow,” Agatha said. “That’s…quite a life.”

“It is what it is,” Alice said, grinning shyly. “It’s nice to spend time together, if nothing else.” 

Agatha nodded, as if understanding the desire to spend time with one’s mother.

“Well, it’s very cool,” Wanda said, smiling. “I hope you have fun and take lots of pictures to show us when you return. When are you leaving?”

“In two weeks,” Alice said. “I know it’s a ways off, but I just wanted you all to be prepared so you don’t miss me too much. Especially this one.” She motioned to Rio, who rolled her eyes.

“I’m sure I’ll manage, Rockstar,” Rio said, nudging Alice’s shoulder with her own. 

“Whatever, Clown,” Alice retorted. “I know you’re going to miss me.”

Rio rolled her eyes again, catching Agatha’s eyes. Agatha felt her cheeks go hot again when Rio smirked, once again pushing her tongue into her cheek.

“I’m sure I’ll find something to occupy my time,” she said, her voice warm and eyes glittery. 

Alice caught her gaze and smirked, nudging Rio’s arm as she shook her head in amusement.

Agatha completely forgot about Jen for the rest of the meeting.

Chapter End Notes

We finally have Rio! And Agatha is feeling some type of way about her. Is it mutual? Maybe.

Comments are very much wanted and lowkey needed. Thanks!

Chapter 4: A Single Mom Who Works Too Hard

Chapter Summary

Rio POV chapter yay!

Chapter Notes

A little Rio backstory with a LOT of exposition. Sorry in advance for all this exposition that I dropped.

I'm not picking it up though...

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Chapter Four

Rio Vidal couldn’t feel her left leg.

Well, that wasn’t entirely true. She could feel it, somewhat , but not enough to walk securely on it for more than a few steps without needing something to keep her upright. Which was why she used a cane that her sister had given her. It had a skull pattern lasered into the wood, along with flowers that she had hand-carved (not enough to disrupt the integrity of the cane, don’t worry) before she gave it to Rio for her thirty-fourth birthday. 

Rio leaned on that cane now as she entered the Westview Community Center’s main room, inhaling the familiar sweaty scent of basketballs and peeling floor wax. She had played basketball here as a kid, the star of her community team, the Pistol Shrimps. She had led them to three straight championships before leaving for college on an athletic scholarship.

Those days were long-gone now.

Alice was already seated with a cup of coffee in her good hand and way too many Oreos in her not-so-good hand. Her hand was only trembling slightly today, though, which was good. Sometimes, Alice couldn’t use it at all. Most days, though, it was just fine. Today was an in-between day.

Rio grabbed a cup of hot water and stuck a green tea bag into the cup, inhaling the hot drink before taking a sip and hobbling to take the seat next to Alice’s. She stole one of her cookies.

“Hey!” Alice whined. “That’s mine!”

“You took way more than your fair share,” Rio retorted, shoving the entire Oreo into her mouth. “Theef arf fer effryfun, Alif,” she said through her mouthful. 

Alice wrinkled her nose. “Don’t speak with your mouth full,” she huffed, holding the rest of her cookies away from Rio as she attempted to snatch another. 

Rio rolled her eyes and chased the cookie with a sip of her tea. “So,” she said, “how’s your mom’s tour going?”

“It’s okay,” Alice said, taking a bite of another cookie. “She wants me to take over for Ralph while he’s on paternity leave.”

“Ralph had a kid?” Rio scoffed. “Who would have a kid with Ralph?”

“His wife?” Alice snorted. 

“Who the hell married that nutcase?!” Rio snorted. “Is she okay? Does she need help?”

“Well, she’s about two minutes from popping out his spawn, so…probably, yeah,” Alice laughed. “I dunno. She seems pretty crazy about him, and he’s been a wreck for the last week, checking his phone constantly. Mom promised to give him four weeks off the tour if he would just get through the next few rehearsals and bring me up to speed on the songs they’re performing. His wife isn’t even due for another month, but apparently Braxton-Hicks are a bitch that have been playing on their last nerves.”

Alice goes on about how much Ralph, her mother’s most recent drummer for her decades-old rock band, has been irritating everybody else with his wife’s pregnancy, not only in the last couple of weeks, but honestly since the pregnancy had been announced.

“This,” Alice said, “is why my mom hates hiring men for the band.”

“Why doesn’t she just bring you on permanently, then?” Rio asked.

Alice held up her trembling hand. “You know why,” she said.

Rio snatched two more cookies and shoved them into her mouth before Alice could even blink, grinning at her, impishly.

“You bitch,” Alice said, part disbelieving and part impressed. Rio waggled her head in victory and teasing as she chewed and swallowed the Oreos down with another gulp of tea.

They continued to chat idly about other stuff as others filed in. There were many familiar faces, of course. They didn’t get many newcomers to the Women’s MS Support Group – it was kind of niche. Some women only came sporadically, whenever they could take the time off caring for their families or when they had the energy, which could be hard to come by with a reasonably progressed disease. But they usually had at least half a dozen familiar faces.

Tonight, including Alice and Rio, they had seven.

Oh. Eight.

Rio’s gaze zeroed in on the woman that walked in, her steps slow and cautious as if she were walking on landmines, her eyes flitting all around her as if something were about to pop out at her. She had on a deep purple sweater with a white collar sticking out of it from beneath. Her jeans, unlike Rio’s, were light and unripped. She had a couple of piercings in her ears, but nowhere near as many as Rio had. Still, they looked good on her. 

Anything would look good on her , Rio thought as she eyed the other woman. She had an athletic build, which was clear even beneath the sweater and jeans, which were both pretty form-fitting (well, at least Rio could clearly see the curve of the woman’s ass, which she was not ogling…it was just… there .) Her dark brown hair was curly and fell loosely around her shoulders, a little messy but it also seemed to be calling for Rio to run her fingers through it.

Her eyes, from what Rio saw when they briefly met hers as the woman glanced around the circle, were the clearest blue she’d ever seen. Rio wanted to drown in them.

“You’re drooling,” Alice whispered in her ear, nudging her and breaking Rio from her thoughts about the beautiful new stranger.

“Shut up,” Rio scoffed, elbowing her gently in the side. “No, I’m not.” She wiped her chin just to be sure.

“She’s pretty,” Alice said, eyeing the newcomer. “You think she’s here for the meeting?”

The woman was now standing at the refreshments table, pouring herself a coffee with oatmilk. She took three Oreos.

“If she’s not, then she’s stealing from us,” Rio joked, but she was unable to really keep her eyes off of the woman as she turned toward the circle and searched for a seat. She took one right across from Rio and Alice. Rio tried not to get caught staring.

The other woman didn’t look at all like she belonged there. Unlike most of the women in the group, she didn’t have any mobility aids or any visibly trembling limbs (like Alice’s hand) or difficulty walking, as far as Rio could see. But that didn’t really mean much. Peggy Carter still walked just fine and she’d gotten her diagnosis half a decade ago. It just depended on how far along the disease had progressed.

And how long one might have ignored the early symptoms.

Rio hadn’t really even noticed the symptoms until it was too late.

She’d noticed her foot falling asleep a lot more often, but she had chalked it up more to poor circulation from her always sitting cross-legged on the floor or bending her knee up against her chest rather than anything she should have been concerned about. She had to just stomp it a few times and ignore the pins and needles, staticky feeling she got that always made her giggle just the tiniest bit when she stood. It only took a few moments and then it was back to normal.

No big deal.

Not until she was pacing across the stage at The Witches’ Road during one of her sets and felt her left leg go numb mid-step. It had come down hard and Rio had stumbled, landing on the hard stage with a thud and a grunt. She could practically see the birds circling above her head as she lay there for a beat too long.

Thankfully, she’d always been quick on her feet (in a manner of speaking) and sat right back up, crossing her legs like she was a little kid in kindergarten. 

“I totally meant to do that,” she said, to nervous laughter from the crowd. “I’m just gonna stay right here, if you all don’t mind. It feels safer.”

Her sister had been in the front row, her eyes going wide with panic when Rio fell. Rio had given her a thumbs up to let her know that she was fine, but she could still see that worry etched in her little sister’s brow for the rest of the show.

Afterward, when nearly all the audience members had left, she had helped Rio to her feet and let Rio lean on her as they made their way to the exit, Rio’s leg still tingling when she got situated in the car. 

At the hospital, she had several scans and blood tests, certain that she just had a blood clot or something. At first, they’d thought she had had a stroke, but she didn’t feel numbness anywhere but in her leg, and only in her lower leg at that. 

They found the lesions on the MRI not long after and referred Rio to a local neurologist, who then referred her to Dr. Lilia Calderu, a sweet old Italian lady with kind brown eyes and an infectious laugh. If Rio had to hear bad medical news, she’d want only Lilia to give it to her from now on. 

Lilia was the one who suggested the MS support group, which was being hosted by a woman named Elektra Natchios at the time. Elektra had been in her sixties and was a wheelchair user. She had established the twice-weekly group five years after her diagnosis as a way to bring together people who were feeling rather isolated by the disease. They all had one thing – or rather, one person – in common though: Lilia Calderu was their doctor.

Lilia had helped Elektra book the community center for her MS group and encouraged other patients to attend Elektra’s group. One of the women who started coming to the group, Carol, even helped make the first brochures for the group to make it look more official. 

Elektra apparently ran the group for the first ten years before she needed to move into a nursing home and leave it fully. By that time, Rio had joined them for about six months and had found a tiny community there. Her sister had come once or twice in the beginning, for support, but Rio had insisted that she didn’t need to be there, that she was fine with her newfound friends.

Alice, in particular, had become one of her fast friends in the group. They were both performers and both the youngest in the group (besides Yelena, who was only there as Natasha’s ride and annoying baby sister) and they had a lot in common as far as their taste in movies and music went. Alice would often attend Rio’s comedy shows on the weekend and give her (unsolicited) notes on which jokes worked best with which crowds. Rio did the same when Alice performed with a local band in Eastview.

They had a good, mutually annoying, partnership.

When Elektra stepped down as the host, they cycled through others (Peggy was the host for a while, then Nat, then Alice was host once) before Wanda officially took up the post. She was thirty-nine at the time and had just started using Lofstrand crutches, but she never complained or seemed to tire out at all. She was a housewife and a mother to twin boys who often showed up to help her set up the refreshments table. They were cute kids, a little rambunctious at times, and super helpful to their mom and the entire group.

Rio couldn’t for the life of her remember their names.

Wanda Maximoff was a ray of sunshine, regardless, and very upbeat and personable. She was the perfect person to lead a support group, in Rio’s opinion. She always kept the energy up throughout each meeting, and her mind was like a steel trap. She would bring up something that somebody had mentioned weeks ago when conversation started to slow down just so that they would never stop talking. 

Rio wondered if she ever got tired.

The other women were great, as well. 

Nat and Yelena were like Tweedle Dee and Tweedle Dum, always bickering about something, either in English or Russian, but they were doing it in an affectionate way. Nat had a similar issue with her right foot as Rio had with her left leg. Rio had once joked that they could kill at a three legged race.

“Or die,” Yelena had interceded.

That had gotten plenty of laughs from the circle around them.

Peggy was their new elder. She was posh and proper and had no children, but she treated them all like they were her kids, even though the youngest among them until now (Rio) was already in her mid-thirties. She still held some kind of authority over the rest of them, even though she wasn’t the host. Even Wanda followed her lead sometimes.

Maria was the newest (again, until today) with a new diagnosis of about a year. She had come to them two months into having the official diagnosis, on recommendation of Peggy, whom she had met at a different group, one for veterans. She was pretty mobile, by her own admission, but she had spells of vertigo from time to time, and headaches that sometimes prevented her from attending the group therapy sessions. She and Peggy were close, since Peggy’s spouse was also a veteran.

In the two years since she started attending the group, Rio had gotten to know all the women there, both the regulars and those who only showed up once or twice a month. She loved making them all laugh during group therapy, and she loved even more making them laugh when they showed up at the club to watch her perform.

She no longer paced the stage during her performances, instead opting to sit on a stool, holding her cane as she made others laugh. And she made others laugh plenty . She loved making others laugh.

Humor was how she and her sister had gotten through their parents’ neglect as kids. It was the most important thing she had. Even losing most of the use of her left leg couldn’t deter her from using humor now.

She found herself even more eager to make this woman in front of her laugh. She looked like she had a great laugh, a great smile. She watched her lips turn up every so often as the others introduced themselves, but mostly she stayed quiet and nodded along to whatever they were saying. She looked like a really good listener, too. 

Goddess , Rio felt ridiculous. She didn’t even know this woman. She was beautiful, sure, and her eyes were the color of a bottomless lagoon, yeah, and her hair was wild and free and looked so soft to the touch and–

“Rio?” 

Rio blinked over at Wanda, who was looking at her expectantly. It was her turn.

Rio nodded and stood, cursing her numb foot as she swayed a little bit. She felt Alice subtly touch her back to stabilize her before pulling away. She cleared her throat and looked directly at the beautiful brunette woman. After all, she was the one they were all introducing themselves to, wasn’t she?

“Hey,” she said, offering a lopsided grin, “my name is Rio Vidal.” She did what the others had done: she introduced herself, how long she’d had her diagnosis, her age, and enough information to hopefully garner the other woman’s interest. 

The brunette definitely seemed interested, which gave Rio a bit of an ego boost. She talked about being a ‘sit-down’ comedian, which earned a small chuckle from the other woman. Rio had been right; she had a ridiculously beautiful laugh. 

When she mentioned that she performed weekly at The Witches’ Road , Wanda added that they tried to go as a group at least once a month. She loved Wanda for that.

Then she called Rio ‘funny girl’ and lost several brownie points for it.

Rio hated to be called ‘funny girl’. It made her sound like a child begging for attention from a group of her parents’ friends and being placated by said friends. This wasn’t that.

She flushed and sat back down as Alice laughed next to her, patting her shoulder.

“Don’t fret,” Alice whispered in her ear. “You haven’t lost her yet.”

Rio shrugged off her hand, but she couldn’t repress her own grin as she glanced over at the brunette, who was still watching her, cheeks slightly pink as she attempted to hide her obvious interest.

Rio’s grin only widened as she stuck her tongue into her cheek, a habit she’d had since she was a little kid and told her first really funny joke that had sent an entire room of adults into hysterics. Her parents’ friends had complimented her on her sense of humor and it had just planted a seed then and there.

Alice introduced herself and talked a little bit about her musical talent (she knew how to play pretty much every instrument thanks to her mom) and mimed playing the drums with the sticks she carried in her back pocket at all times, which earned her a chuckle from the group. Alice also liked to make people laugh, and Rio had to admit that she was pretty funny herself.

Yelena and Nat go next. Yelena doesn’t say much (she never does), but Nat explains their history briefly. Rio had been surprised to learn that they were sisters, but she hadn’t really questioned it after watching them interact for longer than ten seconds. They certainly bickered like sisters. Rio could attest to that.

Then it was the new girl’s turn.

Her cheeks were pink as she stood up, standing straight and tall though she was obviously nervous as she stuck her thumbs into her pockets. She offered the group a half-hearted smile as she introduced herself and her name sparked something in Rio’s brain.

“My name is Agatha Harkness,” she said. “I was diagnosed a little less than a week ago.” 

Rio’s heart clenched at that. She remembered how she felt just after her diagnosis. It was like her world was ending, especially with a leg that refused to work properly. She had only ever heard about MS from celebrities who were already struggling by the time they revealed their diagnoses. It made the disease all the more daunting. She couldn’t imagine how Agatha felt.

“I own The Craft in Westview.”

“The art gallery?” Rio asked, immediately recognizing the name.

It clicked then for Rio. She remembered where she had heard the name. She loved walking through the gallery on days where she just had to think . It wasn’t too large and there were benches in front of most of the bigger installations. She was certain she’d never seen Agatha there, though – that , she definitely would have recalled.

“My partner and I opened it right after grad school,” Agatha explained. Rio felt her heart drop at that.

Partner? So she was taken.

“Oh, I’m twenty-nine, by the way,” Agatha added, flushing.

“A new baby!” Maria exclaimed, clapping her hands. “Rio isn’t the youngest anymore!”

“Happy day,” Rio said, dryly, but one look at Agatha’s face had her smiling again, sticking her tongue into her cheek. Taken or not, Agatha was… something . Rio wanted to know more about her, regardless.

Agatha gave them a little more info about her gallery and Rio found herself asking if she was there often, flirting almost shamelessly despite the fact that Agatha had just mentioned her partner. She really couldn’t help it. It wasn’t like anything would come of it, anyway. Rio was flirty, but she was no homewrecker.

Agatha flushed again, and Rio couldn’t decide if that was because she liked the attention or she was just plain embarrassed by how much Rio was flirting with her. She tried to tamp it down as she listened to Agatha’s response.

She was there during the week, Rio learned.

“Good to know,” she said, trying not to sound flirty. “Maybe I’ll come by.”

“Good,” Agatha replied, then her face immediately went red as she plopped back down in her seat, pulling out her phone. 

Rio just grinned, leaning back in her own seat. Alice nudged her, snorting. Rio nudged back, shaking her head.

Wanda then opened the floor for anything anybody else wanted to talk about. After a long moment of nobody saying anything, Alice mentioned going on tour with her mom’s band (which everybody there knew at this point) and Agatha started asking questions about it, tucking her phone back into her pocket as she leaned forward with interest.

Rio was a little bit jealous of the attention that Alice received at that moment, she couldn’t lie. Objectively, though, it was interesting to be in the same room (and niche support group) as a rock star’s daughter.

Still, Rio wanted those eyes back on her.

She got her wish when Alice made a smart comment about Rio missing her when she was gone. She retorted with something equally smart and felt Agatha’s eyes land on her again. She relished the attention though she tried not to let it show as she and Alice continued to trade teasing quips throughout the rest of the meeting. 

Nat shared about some of her clients (only on long-closed cases) and Peggy talked about the preparations for her upcoming anniversary with her spouse. Rio never could figure out if Peggy was married to a man or a woman. She had always used the term ‘spouse’ rather than husband and wife, and she’d never really used pronouns. She seemed to be very careful about it.

Most of the women in the group, Rio mused as she listened to Maria talk about how frustrating it was to deal with the VA hospital, were some flavor of queer, she realized. Natasha was openly dating women and Yelena had a long-term relationship with the daughter of some elite family that she’d met through work. Maria wore a bisexual pride pin on the lapel of her trench coat and talked about ex-girlfriends with the same casualty as ex-boyfriends. Alice was a textbook lesbian, as was Rio.

She wondered about Agatha. She hadn’t mentioned the gender of her ‘partner’ but she could see Agatha being a lady-lover. Or maybe that was just hopeful thinking. The way she blushed anytime she caught Rio looking at her may have been a good sign.

The meeting ended far too quickly for Rio’s liking, and she watched as Agatha immediately stood and turned toward the door, tossing her long-emptied cup into the trash. Before she could make a full escape, however, Wanda approached her with a wide smile, leaning heavily on her crutches. 

Agatha looked nervous, smiling shyly back at Wanda who was chattering incessantly about how nice it was to see a new face and to let her know if Agatha needed anything . Agatha just nodded, but Rio saw her gaze flit to the exit one too many times. 

“You’re staring again,” Alice whispered in her ear, nudging her in the ribs. “What is it about the new girl that has you acting like this?”

“Acting like what?” Rio huffed, trying to play it cool, but she couldn’t keep her eyes off of Agatha for longer than five seconds.

“Like she’s a cold glass of water,” Alice retorted, “and you’ve been trapped in the desert for years.”

“I would be dead if I was trapped in the desert for years with no water,” Rio retorted. “The human body can’t be without water for longer than three days without death by dehydration.”

“How are you a comedian?” Alice scoffed. “You take everything so literally.”

“Only to annoy you,” Rio retorted affectionately, booping Alice’s nose.

Alice swatted her hand away and Rio laughed, glancing back in Agatha’s direction, only to find Agatha practically speed-walking toward the exit again, Wanda having been distracted by a conversation with Nat and Yelena. 

Rio quickly stood and grabbed her cane, hobbling after Agatha as fast as her three legs could carry her, but by the time she got outside, Agatha was slipping into a custom-pink (because there’s no way it was marketed that way) Subaru with a tall, bald black woman in the driver’s side. The other woman smiled at Agatha and said something, but Agatha just shrugged, looking out the window. She caught Rio’s eye just as the car took off from the sidewalk and exited the lot.

Rio fought the urge to wave goodbye.

“At least you know where she works,” Alice said, appearing from thin air practically. Rio jumped.

“Jesus Christ,” she huffed. “You need to start wearing a bell.” 

Alice just laughed.

 

Rio was met at the door by two furry little freeloaders, meowing and rubbing themselves up against her legs. She chuckled, scratching her gangly black cat behind the ears, relishing in Reaper’s purrs and the way she stretched to meet Rio’s nails. 

Swan was next, barrelling past her sister for love and pets that belied the permanently grumpy expression on her face. She was almost the polar-opposite of her sister: a fat calico with a permanent frown and a brown spot under her nose that looked like a handlebar mustache. But she was super affectionate, especially with Rio.

“Don’t feed them!” a voice rang out from the direction of the kitchen. Her sister appeared, sliding in on the hardwood floor in her socks. “They’ve gotten dinner and three treats already. And they didn’t even hold their end of the bargain by staying still when I was trying to take their photos.” Reaper attempted to atone, rubbing up against Dulce’s legs and purring like a little engine. “Don’t pull that shit with me, Reapy,” Dulce huffed even as she scratched the cat behind her ears. “You’ve been lying down all day to the point where I thought we lost you, but the one time I need you to be still, suddenly you have the zoomies. This will not stand without punishment, you hear me?”

“Mrow,” Reaper said, looking at her with those big green eyes and her own permanent smile. Despite being a mostly black cat, Reaper had white markings all over her little face that looked akin to a human skull. She was like a reverse Siamese.

Rio could see the exact second Dulce broke, her shoulders slumping as she padded back into the kitchen for the container of cat treats. The second she touched it, both cats rushed in after her while Rio laughed, shaking her head. 

She made her way to the couch, kicking off her shoes on the way, and collapsed onto the cushions in the corner of her sectional. Dulce joined her a few moments later, sighing as she practically fell face-first into the cushions.

“Long day?” Rio asked, carding her hand through her little sister’s hair. She scratched her scalp like she’d done when Rio was a little girl and Dulce groaned.

“You have no idea,” she said. “Why are customers the absolute worst?”

“Because capitalism is the worst,” Rio answered. “Pretty soon you’ll be living off your art, though. How much did you get for that one piece you just sold again?”

“Five grand,” Dulce replied, her eyes twinkling as she turned over. “Granted, three of those g’s went to student loans, but that’s still two thousand in the bank. I could afford to take a week off if I wanted.”

“You’re not gonna do that though, are you?” Rio snorted.

Dulce let out a long-suffering sigh. “No,” she said. “We’re short-staffed and Tommy was practically on his knees begging me to stay. It’s not that bad, I guess. I can still misspell people’s names on their cups, and that gives me a tiny bit of joy.”

“Don’t you get proceeds from the prints at that art gallery?” Rio asked, looking down at her sister as Dulce placed her head in Rio’s lap. “Won’t that bring in a little income?”

“I get paid twice a month for that,” Dulce informed her, “and I’m making peanuts . Less than twenty percent from each print sold, and they’re not all selling for 5k no matter how much I wish they would. At least Agatha Harkness complimented me.” Her eyes sparkled at that.

Dulce had been going on and on about the owner of the art gallery complimenting her photo since she got home on Saturday night. Rio couldn’t believe she didn’t recognize the name at her meeting that night until Agatha actually mentioned owning The Craft during her introduction. She tended not to be great with names in passing, though now she was definitely going to remember that one.

She considered, briefly, informing Dulce about who had shown up at her support group meeting, but then she thought better of it. Agatha Harkness wasn’t exactly a celebrity , but she must have been a pretty well-known figure in the community. She owned the only art gallery in town and it was pretty successful, just based on how Dulce described it, at least. 

Rio didn’t know if Agatha wanted the news of her recent diagnosis out there, even just for Rio’s star-struck baby sister.

So when Dulce asked Rio how her meeting went, Rio simply shrugged. “Same old, same old,” she said. “You know. All the usuals.”

“No cute new women for you to seduce with your quick wit?” Dulce teased, grinning up at her.

“If my wit was that quick,” Rio retorted, “I wouldn’t have to spend all week on new stand-up routines.”

“Eh, it’s quick enough,” she said. “I remember high school Rio getting all the chicks.”

“That’s only because I had a cute kid hanging off my leg, charming all the ladies with her dimples.” She poked one dimple now, making Dulce giggle. 

Rio had been twelve years old when her parents gave birth to their second “oops” baby. They really hadn’t meant to have children at all, but Rio had been a prom night accident that they’d decided to half-heartedly raise until she was ‘self-sufficient’ at the ripe age of six, when she got get herself dressed for kindergarten, throw enough into her lunchbox to constitute a relatively-balanced meal, and walk down to wait for the school bus at the corner. They only showed up when they needed to at the school (parent-teacher conferences, school recitals, and moving up ceremonies), but otherwise Rio was raised mainly by babysitters while her parents worked late or enjoyed their twenties. 

Her mother had just turned thirty when the second line appeared on the pregnancy test. They were a little more excited this time around, older and somewhat more mature, ready for another baby. Rio was eleven when they broke the news to her. She tried not to show her disappointment, certain that her parents’ very scant free time will be all used up for the new baby, making her even more neglected than she already was.

She was only kind of wrong.

When Dulce was born (named for the majority of her mother’s cravings, which consisted mainly of chocolate and other kinds of candy and pastries), their parents showered her with love and affection for the first year or so of her life, but Rio was still expected to help. She was taught to change diapers and do feedings all hours of the day – including school nights. It wasn’t long until she was practically a single mother at the age of thirteen, getting Dulce dressed in the mornings before dropping her off at daycare on her way to work.

Dulce’s first word wasn’t mama or dada; it was Rio. 

Well, technically, it was “Wee-oh” but it still upset her parents – especially her mom.

For a few months after that, Rio’s mother spend as much time with Dulce as possible, attempting to get her to say mama or dada and showering her with affection and gifts while still handing her off to Rio for diaper changes. She attempted to get back to breast-feeding, but Rio shut that down. She had just gotten Dulce used to a cuppy in place of a bottle.

Besides, nothing she tried really helped. As far as Dulce knew, the two adults that lived in their house with them were just there . They paid all the bills and the mortgage, but Rio was the one who stayed up with her at night when she had a cough. Rio was the one who sang her lullabies and read her bedtime stories. It was Rio who had taught her how to use the potty and tie her shoes. Rio had pressed kisses to her skinned knee the first time Dulce fell off her tricycle. Rio had clipped her first picture to the refrigerator when Dulce was three and drew a face with a bunch of lines coming out of it.

“Is this the sun?” Rio had gushed in a singsong voice as she placed it on the fridge with a rainbow magnet.

“Spider!” Dulce corrected.

“Oh, silly Riri!” Rio had said, bonking her own head while her baby sister giggled.

Rio had somehow still been able to take part in basketball, toting Dulce with her to practices and games. Dulce had been a good kid, sitting with her juice and Goldfish and a coloring book in the stands while Rio and her teammates had run drills. She’d sat with some of Rio’s friends during games (her parents rarely attended anything they didn’t need to be at) and cheered whenever Rio made a basket. Afterward, Rio would teach her how to play the game, even though Dulce was just barely bigger than the basketball she held in her tiny hands. Rio would pick her up and help her make a basket.

She did get a lot of attention from girls from toting around an adorably-dimpled toddler after basketball games. They’d buy Dulce milkshakes and french fries at the local diner after games, cooing at her and telling her how cute she was and how much she looked like her big sister – which she did. Dulce was practically Rio’s little mini-me. 

As college approached, Rio was offered several scholarships from the bigger universities for her basketball prowess, but the thought of going as far as UCLA to play made her stomach clench. She just couldn’t imagine being that far away from Dulce.

Instead, she went to a nearby state school, commuting all four years and making enough time to do Dulce’s typical bedtime routine. She never wanted anything to change too drastically for the little girl.

Her parents took over a little bit while she was in college (minimally) and couldn’t spend all her time making sure that Dulce was getting her homework done or getting her nutrients or going to be on time. They acted like a fun uncle and aunt, taking her out on fun excursions and letting her eat way too much junk food that made her sick (which they left Rio to deal with) and even attended a few of Dulce’s parent-teacher conferences (which Rio had previously attended to, telling the teachers that her parents worked long hours in the past) and her mother was practically beaming with pride when she told Rio that they wanted to place Dulce on an advanced track.

“They said she’s doing so well in all her subjects, my little dulcita ,” she cooed, pinching Dulce’s cheek. Dulce shrugged her off, shyly, still not familiar with her mother’s affection. She hated being called ‘dulcita’. Of course, her mother didn’t know that. How could she?

“Are you a smarty-pants?” Rio had cooed, hoisting her six-year-old sister onto her hips and poking her in the belly. “Are you a baby genius?”

“I’m not a baby!” Dulce had insisted. “I’m a big girl! I can ride a two-wheeler now.”

“Ooh, that’s right,” Rio said, as if she’d just remembered. “I totally forgot. Okay, well, do you want to go into the smarty-pants classes? Is that something you might enjoy?”

“I dunno…” Dulce had said, pressing her face into Rio’s shoulder.

“You don’t know?” Rio gasped, tickling her until she squealed. “Well, do you wanna learn more stuff at school?”

“Yeah…” Dulce said. “But what if the other kids don’t like me? What if they think I’m annoying?”

Dulce was only in first grade, but there were a lot of kids who knew her as a know-it-all because she absorbed information so quickly, like a sponge. They made fun of her for it.

“Well, that’s the best part!” Rio cooed, carrying her to the fridge and taking out a juice pouch for her. “This is a special class with kids that are just like you .”

“Juice?” Dulce asked, her eyes wide. It was after five already, and Rio never let her have sugar after five.

Of course that would be the thing she focused on.

“Of course, Miss Smarty Pants,” Rio said. “You deserve a treat for being so smart, and sugar helps the brain be smart – but only in moderation.” Dulce nodded, making grabby hands for the juice. Rio stuck the straw in and handed it over, placing a kiss on her temple as she sat her down at the kitchen table. Her parents had already wandered elsewhere in the house, resigned to the knowledge that Rio had all of Dulce’s attention now, as always.

“So,” Rio said, sitting down next to her, “do you wanna do the smarty-pants class?”

“I get to choose?” Dulce asked, brown eyes wide with wonder. She so rarely had a choice in things like this, outside what she wore in the morning and what songs Rio sang her at night.

“Of course you do,” Rio said. “This is going to be your everyday life, isn’t it? You’re the one who is gonna have to go to school every day and learn with the other kids. And they’re all gonna be smarty-pantses, too.”

“They are?” Dulce asked around her straw.

“Of course,” Rio said, slipping her laptop out of her bag to subtly start her homework. “They’re all gonna be in the smarty-pants class, so they’re all gonna be smarty-pantses.”

“That makes sense,” Dulce said, sipping her juice. “Do you think they’ll wanna be my friends?”

“I don’t know why they wouldn’t be,” Rio said, softly, reaching out to push a lock of Dulce’s wavy black hair behind her ear. “You are such a cool kid.”

“The kids in my other class don’t like me,” Dulce muttered, looking down.

“Hey,” Rio said, tucking a finger under her chin to make her look her in the eye. “It’s not that they don’t like you,” she said. “It’s that they don’t understand you. These kids will, okay?”

“Okay,” Dulce said. “Then I’ll do it.”

“That’s my girl!” Rio exclaimed, grabbing her in a hug and pressing kisses to her dimpled cheeks. “Now, what do you want for dinner?”

It had been as simple as that. Dulce had gotten into the accelerated program, which was probably the best decision they’d ever made. By the time Rio was graduating undergrad (on crutches because of an ACL tear at her very last game, which was amazing timing), Dulce was graduating junior high at only ten years old. By fourteen, she was in college, where she finally slowed down a little bit – but not by that much.

When Rio was twenty-five, she received her master’s in botany and was considering a PhD when her parents decided to kick her to the curb, claiming that she was far too old to be living at home. 

Dulce obviously rioted at this. Rio had never seen her sister throw a fit quite like the one Dulce threw at the notion of her beloved older sister being forced to move out of their shared childhood home. She was thirteen years old at that point, but she acted like a toddler, knocking things off of shelves and stomping her foot and screaming at her parents the way she never had.

Naturally, their parents blamed Rio for this, telling her that she’d been a bad influence, that she had spoiled Dulce to a ridiculous extent.

Rio had laughed in their faces at that, pointing out that, because of her, Dulce was about to graduate high school with honors and already had several scholarships to get her through both a bachelor’s and master’s degree of her own. She reminded them who exactly had changed Dulce’s diapers, had fed her, had taken her to doctor’s appointments, had dropped her off and picked her up from school, had acted as a mother to their child while they were off doing whatever the fuck they wanted to do. Rio had been the strict one in the household, setting bedtimes and curfews while they had encouraged her to eat too much candy and stay up late, getting in the way of her studies. 

“Then take her with you!” her mother had roared at her, stunning both Dulce and Rio into silence.

Rio and Dulce had just looked at her, then each other, slow smiles spreading over their lips.

“Fine,” Rio had said. “I will.”

The process to take legal guardianship over her younger sister hadn’t been as difficult as she’d been expecting. Her parents paid child support and Rio found a job as a florist and an apartment for them to share, for which her parents also paid half the rent. She had expected her mother to take back her words almost as soon as they were spoken, but her parents were surprisingly willing to let Dulce go with her, even to throw money at them to make it easier, so long as they no longer had to care for them.

Rio had put Dulce into therapy as soon as possible despite the teen insisting that she didn’t need it. Rio didn’t believe her and it was covered by their insurance, so it wasn’t like she’d be wasting money on it. 

Dulce saw her therapist for a year during her freshman year in college, but she quit therapy in the summer between freshman and sophomore year, insisting that it was only taking time away from her studies and her photography hobby, which really started to pick up after she took a photography class during her spring semester.

As with most things, Dulce was a prodigy. It took her no time at all to learn how to use the expensive, artsy cameras that the university lent out for their classes, and pretty soon her work was featured in the university’s tiny student-run art gallery and their art magazine. Rio stuck those articles on the fridge in their apartment, much to her teen sister’s embarrassment. 

Before long, Dulce graduated college at age eighteen, the same age as the incoming freshman, and moved to New York to attend grad school at Columbia, where she got her MFA in Photography. She still came home every weekend to spend time with Rio and support her as she started performing at open mics around New Jersey. 

When Rio got a steady gig at The Witches’ Road , Dulce made a point to be there every single week to see her show. She’d only missed a handful so far, including the previous Saturday. She had to practically force Rio not to cancel her standup for her showcase.

“You can go to the gallery anytime to see the prints they’ve got in the gift shop. Hell, if I don’t sell the original, you can see that, too! It’s no big deal.”

Rio had grumbled a lot about it, but she’d gone to her show, talking about raising an ungrateful brat, but in an affectionate way. She’d made Alice take a video of it to show Dulce when she got home, receiving that playful pout from her baby sister. Dulce had then raved about the owner of the gallery (Rio could swear she’d never said Agatha’s name) complimenting her piece and her talent and assured Rio it definitely wouldn’t be her last showcase there.

Rio was certain of that, too. 

“So,” Dulce said, as she sat upright on the couch, welcoming Swan into her lap, where she immediately stretched out across her legs, “when are you going on tour?”

Rio scoffed. “Why would I do that? I’ve got a nice regular gig in Westview and an irritating little sister to look after.”

“I’m not a baby, Rio,” Dulce huffed, crossing her arms over her chest. “I can handle myself for a few weeks while you go out and make some real money.”

“I make plenty at The Witches’ Road ,” Rio insisted.

“Your TikToks are blowing up, you know,” Dulce said, sliding her phone out of her pocket to show her. “There are a lot of lesbians eager to meet the sexy gay comedian girl, but they’re all stuck in the midwest. You could get a lot of–”

“Watch your mouth,” Rio said, using her hand to muffle her words. Dulce glared at her and licked her palm. 

Rio immediately recoiled. “Ugh!” she exclaimed. “I rue the day I ever taught you that.” She wiped her hand on Dulce’s sleeve and Dulce shoved her away, laughing.

“Come on, Riri!” Dulce groaned. “Don’t you wanna branch out a little bit. There is probably a world of other plant lesbians who love lame puns just as much as you do.” She motioned to Rio’s shirt, which was one of many with similar dirty plant jokes. “You could get laid so much if you just left Westview for a little bit.”

“I’m fine for now,” Rio chuckled. “I have everything I need right here.” She wrapped her arms around Dulce, pulling her into a tight embrace. Dulce sighed but didn’t resist for even a second, pressing her cheek to Rio’s shoulder.

“I’m not letting this go, you know,” she said, even as she relaxed. 

“I know,” Rio said, patting her head and grinning as she reached for the remote. “So what are we watching?”

Chapter End Notes

Reminder that Dulce is played by Jenna Ortega in my brain.

ALSO the calico cat's full name isn't Swan. Can anybody guess what her full name is? I may have dropped a few hints throughout.

Chapter 5: Azaleas

Chapter Summary

Agatha goes on her usual run and runs into a surprise - but not an unwelcome one.

Jen has an "emergency" 😒

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Five

Agatha didn’t go to work on Tuesday.

It wasn’t like she was needed there, anyway. Their next showcase wasn’t for another two weeks, and Jen was handling all the logistics of this one. She was probably already sitting in her office, making phone calls and whatnot. She was definitely still texting Agatha about the previous night’s group therapy. 

Agatha ignored the chime of her phone strapped to her arm as she ran through the park, keeping her breaths even and in time with her steps as her feet pounded the pavement. It was nice out and she was going to enjoy this run, dammit.

While she still could.

Agatha shook off that thought, literally feeling her ponytail whack her in the face as she kept going. She focused on the path in front of her, on dodging walkers with dogs and parents pushing strollers. She waved at a little girl who waved at her as she passed, offering her a kind smile and a goofy face that made her giggle. She jumped over a branch in the middle of the path, never breaking her stride. She weaved around a kid attempting to do some kind of trick on his skateboard. She brushed shoulders with another runner as she passed him and ignored the flirty smile he sent her way, pointing to the lesbian pride flag design of the armband keeping her phone in place.

Luckily, he was smart enough not to pursue…or maybe he just wasn’t quick enough to catch up.

The park wasn’t huge. Agatha didn’t think it even stretched a quarter-mile from one end to the other in any direction, but the paths that had been built around it and going through it were windy and split in many different directions. Agatha probably wouldn’t have been surprised to learn that she’d never taken the same jog twice through the park. 

There was a playground that she passed sometimes. It was small, but it almost always had kids playing on it, even early in the morning, before school. There was also a school right across the street from the park, so the playground was probably an extension of that. 

There was a duck pond where people had picnics in the early afternoons, tossing seeds and breadcrumbs to the duck families swimming and waddling around. They were loud, but at least they didn’t poop on the path as far as Agatha could see.

There were benches everywhere in the park, dotting the path. They were mostly taken up by the elderly, but there were also some younger couples who sat together, holding hands and being gross. Agatha did her best to ignore them and the thought that it had been well over a year since she’d really had a date, let alone a girlfriend.

But that thought brought her back to the previous night, to the room full of women. To Rio .

All of the women in that room were unfairly hot, but Rio Vidal was…something else. And she had kept staring at Agatha.

That had to mean something, didn’t it?

Agatha hoped that something was positive.

And she’d said that she would maybe drop by. 

And then Agatha had said good. Good .

Agatha groaned at the memory, her stride stuttering just slightly before she picked up speed. She checked her smartwatch and saw that she had been running for 4.65 miles so far. It was about time to wrap up and head home, she decided. She’d end at five miles and call it a day.

Well, a morning . It wasn’t even ten yet.

She took the next path to her left, in the direction of her house, and kept going, checking her watch every few moments, watching the distance tick higher and higher as she reached the edge of the park. 

Unfortunately, she knew she would reach the sidewalk before she reached her goal, so she took a sharp right onto an arching path that would tack on a good .05 miles onto her run and give her the extra distance she needed to reach her - 

“Agatha?”

Agatha jumped a little at the sound of her name and she turned to see none other than Rio Vidal grinning at her. She was dressed similarly to how she’d been the night before, in torn jeans (these ones were lighter and baggier) and a deep green shirt with another plant pun on it.

“Pot Head” it said, pictured with a series of drawings of potted plants on it, all with suggestively red eyes. Agatha snorted at it automatically, then cleared her throat.

“Hey,” she said, feeling her cheeks warm up. “Rio, right?” As if she’d actually forgotten the name of the woman she hadn’t been able to stop thinking about since the previous name. The woman she had practically sprinted from the second the meeting was over (after Wanda let her go, anyway).

“Yeah,” Rio laughed, and that sound . How did a laugh sound like music? It was such a casual sound, too, but beautiful. Agatha wanted to drown in it. “Rio.”

Oh Goddess , when did she become a poet? She hated poetry. She was not a writer. She couldn’t understand words as well as paintings, as drawings, as photographs. A picture was worth a million words in her opinion.

“What brings you to the park, Rio?” Agatha asked, jogging in place. It wouldn’t make her mile counter go up, but at least the steps would.

“Community garden,” Rio retorted, motioning to the green area just inside the curving path, which was a tiny flower garden, with vibrant coloring. “I was just checking on my azaleas. They’re coming along well, I think.”

Agatha almost stopped jogging in place at that. She was the one who grew the azaleas in the park? Agatha loved those flowers. She took a detour past them whenever she was having a crappy morning. They were not quite in bloom just yet, but they still looked good and healthy, their stems a vibrant green. The bush took up a third of the tiny patch of land, sitting next to a patch of…well, Agatha didn’t really know other flowers, to be honest. 

Azaleas had been her grandmother’s favorite, so they were also Agatha’s favorite, if only because they reminded her of Gran.

“They do look nice,” Agatha agreed, still jogging in place, hoping that the flush that she could feel spreading from her neck to the tips of her ears could be played off as a result of her physical activity. “How long have you been growing them?”

Rio shrugged, casually. “Since undergrad,” she said. “We had an assignment in my first botany class to grow a flower. That was it. That was the whole assignment.” Rio laughed, her cheeks turning pink. “I went to the hardware store and there was a pack of discounted seeds there. The guy told me that they were from a bad batch and were just trying to get rid of them, but he didn’t think the flowers they’d produce would really turn into anything.”

“I’m guessing,” Agatha said, looking at the flowers, “you took that as a challenge.”

“Oh, one hundred percent,” Rio chuckled, crossing one leg over the other and subtly massaging her ankle. She was wearing white Keds with little doodles sketched on the sides in pen. It made Agatha smile slightly. “I spent so much time on that first batch of azaleas, knowing that I would probably fail my assignment if they never even sprouted.”

“But they did?” Agatha asked.

“Yeah,” Rio said, nodding, “but they were not pretty. Not like these.” She motioned to the currently rooted azalea bush. “I got a good grade, though. Mainly because I took good notes and photographed every step of the process of growing the flowers. My professor was impressed when she saw what the seeds had looked like, too. She said getting even one bud from that was a feat in and of itself. But I got six perfectly fine flowers.”

“Impressive,” Agatha said. At some point, she’d stopped jogging in place and now stood, her arms folded over her chest as she listened to Rio’s story. She had a soothing voice and an electric look in her eyes as she talked about it. “I never would have taken you for a botany student.”

“Really?” Rio snorted, motioning to her shirt. “ Never ?”

“Well, a plant girl, no doubt,” Agatha laughed. “And you’re a comedian, so the puns on your shirt are definitely to be expected, but you studied botany ? Did you ever get your PhD?”

“I was going to,” Rio said, “but life had other plans.” She shrugged but didn’t elaborate. “I’m doing pretty well for myself now, though. I still get to work with plants sometimes, in my own time. I even sell them on occasion. And I get to spend my weekends making people laugh, so it’s a win-win.”

“I suppose so,” Agatha hummed, nodding.

“What about you?” Rio asked. “Did you ever think about getting a PhD?”

“In Art?” Agatha snorted. “Not really. A Master’s in Art History was enough to open the gallery. Making myself an art doctor just felt like overkill.”

“What about your partner?” Rio asked, reaching for a styrofoam coffee cup that Agatha hadn’t previously seen and taking a sip. Agatha wasn’t too concerned by that, though. She was more focused on the emphasis Rio had placed on the word ‘partner’. It was subtle, but Agatha was certain she hadn’t imagined it.

Then it clicked.

Rio thought that Jen was…no. 

Absolutely not.

That was one time and Jen still hadn’t let her live it down!

Never again.

Ew.

“Jen did actually get her PhD,” Agatha said, trying to sound casual and not inject any affection into Jen’s name. It really wasn’t that hard. “But that’s only because her parents were paying for everything and she also got herself a degree in chemistry so she could make her own paint. Overachiever, that one.” Agatha snorted.

“How’d you two meet?” Rio asked, and Agatha could swear she heard an edge to her voice. She wasn’t doing the best job at convincing Rio that Jen was just a partner. A business partner. Not a life partner – gross.

“College,” Agatha said, and thought better than to elaborate on their one-night stand and then her ghosting Jen until graduation. It definitely wouldn’t help her case.

Why did she care if Rio thought she and Jen were an item?

She didn’t.

Did she?

Agatha bit back a groan.

“Um, we met during junior year, both studying art history. After undergrad, we started spending more time together and eventually found ourselves in similar classes in grad school. Jen was studying more chemistry to ‘enhance’ her art,” Agatha rolled her eyes, “but I was also taking a few business classes. After grad school, we decided to put our brains – and our respective bank accounts – together and buy the gallery downtown. It took a lot of fixing up, but it’s been doing well for the last five years, and Jen lives close to it in case of any emergencies.”

Agatha tried to subtly emphasize the fact that she and Jen definitely weren’t living together to Rio, her eyes widening slightly for about half a second before she cleared her throat with a soft shrug. She couldn’t tell if it was getting through to Rio at all, but she didn’t want to come right out and say that she had no romantic feelings for Jen Kale at all.

Because why would that matter?

Rio just nodded. “Pretty impressive,” she said. “Running a successful art gallery right out of grad school, I mean.”

“Well, technically,” Agatha said, “I still had to wait another year or two until Jen was done with her PhD. We did buy the place immediately, but there was a lot of construction that had to be done, as well. It was not in good shape when we signed the papers. Jen’s parents more or less bankrolled us for the duration of the construction.” She took a deep breath. “I’m still paying them back for it all.”

They refused to accept whatever money Agatha tried to give them, so she ended up just slipping wads of cash into Naomi Kale’s purse whenever she showed up to whisk Agatha and Jen off for a nice lunch.

Naomi never said anything, but she always showed up with really nice gifts the next time she came around. They were always suspiciously worth exactly what Agatha had given her. 

Agatha was not allowed to refuse them.

It was a fun game they played.

“Well, you must be doing well,” Rio said. “My sister loves that gallery. Actually, she just–”

Agatha’s phone chirped in her pocket at that second and she gave Rio an apologetic smile as she reached for it, unstrapping it from her arm to see a text from Jen, as if her fucking ears had been burning or something.

“Speak of the she-devil,” Agatha sighed, reading the text that Jen had sent it. It read: “SOS. 911. EMERGENCY! COME QUICK! NOW!” in several different blue bubbles, with another on the way, the little ellipses doing a wave as Jen constructed the next text. “Okay,” Agatha said, eyes wide. “I am so sorry, but I actually have to go. Jen is panicking about… something . I’m,” she looked back at Rio, seriously reluctant to leave this conversation until she was certain that Rio knew that she and Jen were not an item.

But Jen was about to have a heart attack, it seemed. So…

“Go ahead,” Rio said. “I’ll see you at the next meeting.”

It wasn’t a question. It was a statement, like Agatha’s presence at the next group therapy session was a given, a sure thing. Something even glimmered in Rio’s eyes – something like hope . 

Agatha didn’t want to read too much into it, but that glimmer made her heart flutter.

“Yes,” she said, strapping her phone back to her arm. “Yes, definitely. I’ll see you at the next meeting.” She nodded, clearing her throat. “Bye, Rio.”

“Bye, Agatha,” Rio replied, giving her a lopsided smirk, that adorable little gap showing between her front teeth. Agatha wondered what it would be like to run her tongue–

Her phone chirped several more times and she rolled her eyes, waving to Rio with a bemused smile as she started to jog away, toward the gallery.

She could feel Rio’s eyes on her back practically until she left the park.

 

“What’s wrong?” Agatha asked, bursting through the door to Jen’s office. 

It was a split office. It had once been a large room in the gallery, near the reception desk, but Agatha and Jen had decided that it was big enough to split down the middle into two smaller offices. One of the offices (Agatha’s) had a private bathroom, which Jen had been upset about losing in a game of rock, paper, scissors, while the other had windows. Agatha liked not having windows in her office, though. She didn’t need people walking down Main Street seeing inside her office. Gross.

Jen liked her windows, which were floor-to-ceiling and faced East, so she could do yoga in the mornings she’d shown up early, basking in the rising sun.

Agatha had not hesitated in calling her a freak for this.

Now, the sun filtered through the windows behind Jen’s desk as she sat there, completely composed, sipping from a hot pink coffee mug.

“Good morning, Agatha,” she said, coolly.

“Good morning?” Agatha huffed, shutting the door behind her. “ Good morning ?! Jen, what’s with all the SOS and ‘emergency’ texts? You made it seem like you were dying or something?”

“And it still took you ten minutes to arrive,” Jen tutted. “I could have been dead on the floor of a bullet wound by the time you got here.”

“I trust that you would have had enough sense to call 9-1-1 before that happened,” Agatha said, rolling her eyes. “And you texted me right in the middle of my run.”

That was technically a lie, but Jen didn’t need to know that. She couldn’t see the park from her office, thankfully.

“Also, who the hell owns a gun in Westview that you could annoy enough to shoot you in the middle of a crowded gallery? These walls aren’t soundproof, you know.”

“Still,” Jen said, standing up and walking slowly and methodically around her desk as Agatha watched her. “I could have been in real peril and you just left me here, hanging.”

“I ran as fast as I could.”

Another lie. Agatha could have run faster if she’d tried. They both know that. Her mile record was just under five minutes (but that had left her unable to run for nearly a week due to the soreness in her legs.)

“Can you just tell me what you want,” Agatha huffed. “I need to go home and shower. It’s supposed to be my day off, too. I was going to paint.”

“You can leave,” Jen said, still talking as slowly as she walked to the door to her office, “as soon as you tell me what happened at the therapy thing last night.” Agatha heard the click of the door’s lock.

“You think I won’t throw myself out that window?” Agatha huffed, motioning to one of the windows behind the desk. “Because I will. I will take a running leap, Jennifer.”

“Don’t be so dramatic,” Jen huffed, rolling her eyes.

“ Me ?” Agatha sputtered. “ Dramatic? I have nearly forty texts from you, all with some variation of ‘SOS’ and ‘Save me!’ because what? You wanted gossip ?”

Chapter Six

Agatha dressed more casually for the second meeting. 

She still wore unripped jeans, but she’d opted for a t-shirt with a floral pattern that looked as though it were drawn in chalk. It was a little bit faded and Agatha couldn’t tell you what flowers were drawn on it, but she’d bet that Rio could.

Not that it mattered if Rio could, of course. Not that she was even trying to get Rio’s attention with the shirt. 

Shut up.

Her hair was hastily put up into a messy bun that somehow looked almost elegant, even though there were tendrils falling out all over the place. In the mirror, she looked almost put together. Almost.

Sisters.

Maybe.
