“Awww, Aggie!” she practically squealed, reaching to tug Agatha against her side as she came to a stop at a red light. “Ya big softy!”

“Ugh!” Agatha growled. “Let me go, freak!” Agatha said, shoving away from her and pressing herself against the door, glaring at Jen. “You never heard me say that. You will repeat it to nobody or I’ll tell Alice that you still wear a retainer.”

“Fine,” Jen snorted. “But just know that I know that you actually like having me around.”

“I plead the Fifth,” Agatha muttered, looking out the window. “And I will still throw myself out of this moving car so you better shut up.”

“So dramatic,” Jen murmured, but she was still grinning. “I love you, Ags,” she said quietly. Then, “as an annoying little sister, of course.”

“I’m older than you,” Agatha shot back.

“I meant that literally, in terms of size,” Jen said.

Agatha pressed her lips together.

“Say it back,” Jen egged, reaching over to poke her in the cheek.

“No,” Agatha grumbled, swatting her hand away.

“Come onnnn,” Jen said. “You know you want to. You know you feel it, too. Say it back.”

“Fuck off,” Agatha huffed, pressing even further against the car door, trying to evade Jen’s perfectly manicured fingers. Her arms were freakishly long.

“Just say it!” Jen whined. “Come on! Don’t be such a baby!”

“If I say it, will you promise to stop bugging me?” Agatha snapped.

“Of course,” Jen replied easily, batting her long lashes at her.

Agatha rolled her eyes and grumbled something.

“Oh, that doesn’t count!” Jen scoffed. “You have to say it clearly.”

“Ughhhh,” Agatha groaned. “ Fine . Jen, I love you like an annoying, freakishly tall, bougie-as-fuck sister. Happy?”

Jen pressed her lips together for a long moment as she drove, then shrugged.

“It’ll do.”

Chapter End Notes

Next chapter will be from Rio's POV because I feel like we need more of those.

Also, I am STRUGGLING to come up with a comedy set for her. If anybody is good at stand-up comedy and wants a writing credit, please help.

Chapter 7: Ice Breakers

Chapter Summary

Rio POV Chapter about her not-date with Agatha. This is something out of a romantic comedy, I swear.

Chapter Notes

CONTENT WARNING: I mention a brief relationship between 14yo Dulce and 17yo "Johnny Walker" (yes this is John Walker, which is why I later refer to him as Walker rather than Johnny) which is based on something that happened with my own sister (who was a freshman, not a 14yo senior at the time) and still creeps me out to this day. It's meant to be creepy. I just want to put that out there that I do not support that shit.

I just wanna continue to slander John Walker's name.

Otherwise, this chapter has fluff, angst, and tension.

If you see spelling/grammar errors, no you don't.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Chapter Seven

Rio’s bedroom faced east.

Most mornings, she despised this fact, but Dulce had won the west-facing bedroom fair and square – via an intense game of Connect Four that ended with Rio lying facedown on the kitchen floor.

On mornings when she had to – or rather, wanted to – be up early, she was thankful for the rising sun peeking through her crappy blackout curtains. The bright light woke her up much better than any alarm ever could. The sunbeams hit her right in the face the second day broke, so she was up and usually unable to go back to sleep.

On any other morning, that would have been entirely unwelcome.

This morning, however, that would have been perfect. The sun was meant to rise at a quarter to seven, giving her more than an hour to get ready to see Ag–ahem, her azaleas. Yes, her precious, beautiful azaleas that she had been taking care of for years and would hopefully continue to take care of well into her old age. 

She watered them every three days if there was no rain to do it for her. And they’d been without rain for at least a week now, so Rio was certain that–

“ Fuck ,” Rio growled when her alarm woke her up that morning instead of the rising sun. 

It was seven AM. There was no sun peeking through her curtains. 

Rio felt an anxious churning in her stomach as she pushed herself out of bed and hobbled the couple of feet to her window on the opposite wall. She tugged open the curtains and groaned aloud when she saw the steady downpour of rain hitting the street below.

Punctuated by lightning.

“Fuck you,” Rio grumbled at the faraway strike of lightning. Thunder answered her and she flipped it off – as well as one could flip off thunder, anyway.

Fucking Thor.

Rio let out a loud groan as she hobbled back to her bed and flopped down facefirst, groaning obscenely loud into her comforter.

There was a knock on her door. 

“You okay?” Dulce asked, sounding far too awake for seven in the morning.

Rio grumbled something unintelligible (even to herself) into her mattress.

“Okay, I’m coming in,” Dulce said, seconds before the door opened. “What’s wrong?” she asked, eyeing Rio kneeling on the ground with her face on the blankets, hair a wild mess around her head, shoulders limp with defeat.

Swan and Reaper slinked into the bedroom, the latter immediately jumping onto the bed and stretching herself over Rio’s pillows while the former preferred to loaf her plump body onto Rio’s calves and immediately fall back asleep.

Rio, unable to stand up due to Swan’s asshole behavior, turned her head in Dulce’s direction. “I was supposed to go to the park today,” she said, in a pitiful tone, “to water my azaleas.”

Dulce glanced toward the window. “It’s raining,” she pointed out, unhelpfully.

“Thank you, Baby Einstein,” Rio grumbled. “I see that.”

“So Mother Nature took care of the watering for you,” Dulce said, raising one brow. “Why are you upset?”

“Because I wanted to go to the park,” Rio whined like a child. “I was going to be productive today.”

“You can still be productive,” Dulce pointed out. “Just go for a walk in the rain. Wear your poncho and your grippy shoes, though. I swear to God, Rio, if I get a call from the ER again…”

“That was one time ,” Rio grumbled, “and I told that lady I didn’t need an ambulance. I didn’t even have a scrape!”

“People see the cane and lose all brain cells,” Dulce sighed, padding over to sit next to Rio on the floor. She reached for Swan, ignoring her disgruntled mews as she pulled her into her lap and started cooing at her, placating her with scritches under her chin.

Rio gave her a thankful smile as she turned her body so that she could sit cross-legged on the floor. She laid her head down on Dulce’s shoulder. Dulce mirrored her position, the two of them petting Swan, who was purring contentedly across their laps.

Not to be left out, Reaper slinked over to them and draped herself over their heads in a way that could definitely not be ignored. Rio snorted, shaking her head as she reached up to scratch the cat on her head. Reaper chirped happily.

“So why are you actually upset?” Dulce asked after a long moment of just enjoying the purring of their two fur babies.

“Hmm?” Rio hummed, avoiding her gaze as she took Reaper into her arms, holding her like a baby in her arms as she scratched her belly. Reaper nipped at her fingers playfully.

“Don’t pretend you didn’t hear me,” Dulce huffed. “Why are you actually upset that you can’t go to the park? Just because you can’t water your plants? Or is it something else ?” Dulce squinted her eyes at Rio, searching her gaze.

Rio, for her part, tried very hard not to blink or look away or lose the unaffected facade she was desperately trying to put on under her baby sister’s scrutinizing gaze.

But nobody wins a blinking contest with Dulce Vidal. Nobody.

“Jesus!” Rio huffed. “Don’t your eyes ever dry out?” She blinked several times in succession and rubbed at her eyes.

“Nope!” Dulce said, chipperly. “Now spill: who is she?”

“What?” Rio snorted. “Who is whom?”

“Don’t try to dazzle me with your correct use of whom,” Dulce said, now pointing in Rio’s face. “What. Is. Her. Name?” She poked her nose to punctuate each word.

“I don’t know who you’re talking about,” Rio said, swatting her hands away and trying to play dumb as she slung Reaper’s body on her shoulder and pushed herself to her feet. She sat down on her bed as she continued to stroke the lanky cat’s back. Reaper trilled, stretching her legs a little bit as she relaxed more into Rio’s shoulder.

“Riri,” Dulce groaned, standing up with the heavy block of Swan’s weight in her arms, “come oooon. It’s me . Your sister. Sisters don’t keep secrets from each other.” 

“Like your high school boyfriend who was way too old for you?” Rio shot back. “What was his name…Johnny?”

Dulce’s face blanched. “You know about Johnny?” she asked.

“I caught the idiot trying to sneak in during your senior year,” Rio said. “You know, when you were fourteen and he was seventeen? He tried selling me your AP Calc textbook. The one where your name was clearly written on it on a piece of packing tape on the back.”

“Fucking dumbass,” Dulce muttered. “That only lasted, like, three weeks,” she admitted. “And nothing happened other than kissing, I swear. I was tutoring him in math and…God, he was so dumb, but kinda hot.”

“Gross.”

“Whatever, lesbian,” Dulce huffed. “Not the point. Stop deflecting. Just tell me who she is.”

Rio groaned, stroking Reaper’s fur as the cat lounged on her shoulder and made biscuits with her back fat. “Just this woman who runs in the park sometimes,” she said, offering a half-truth. She didn’t want to reveal too much just yet. She still didn’t know how Agatha might feel about people knowing her diagnosis so soon. Rio knew that Dulce would never tell anybody, of course, but still.

Dulce would also give her so much shit for not telling her a) that she had met an attractive newcomer to the therapy group and b) that said woman was her favorite artist and gallery owner, Agatha freaking Harkness.

She didn’t want to deal with that just yet, though logically she knew it was coming sooner or later.

“And have you spoken to this woman?” Dulce asked, rocking Swan in her arms like a big fat baby. “Or do you just watch her like a creep from behind your azalea bush?”

“Of course I’ve spoken to her,” Rio huffed. “Quite a few times, actually.”

“Really? What’s her name? What is she like?” Dulce jumped up, getting a startled mrow from Swan as she bounced onto the bed next to Rio, looking down at her with excited brown eyes.

“She’s…funny,” Rio said. “She likes my punny shirts.”

She looked down at the shirt that she’d worn yesterday, which she didn’t bother to take off before bed. Agatha had snorted at it and Rio felt like the funniest motherfucker in the world.

“Oh, wow,” Dulce said. “You better not let her slip through your fingers. That’s a rare one.”

“Fuck off, my shirts are hilarious,” Rio snorted, shoving her shoulder.

“Gentle,” Dulce said, when Swan let out a pitiful meow in protest of the rough movement, “I am with child.”

Rio rolled her eyes. “Anyway,” she said, “enough about me and my pathetic crush. Why are you up and dressed so early? Early shift?”

“Bold of you to assume I ever went to bed last night,” Dulce said, letting out a yawn. One that Swan echoed. “I was up last night editing my last batch of photos for Sharon’s group.”

“Ooh, how are they coming?” Rio asked. “Any with me in them? You can earn big money with my beautiful face, you know.”

“Yeah, you can’t even tell it’s you in the shot I sold,” Dulce reminded her. “And no, they’re mostly city shots from the tops of buildings around Westview.”

“How the hell did you gain roof access to buildings around Westview?” Rio asked, raising one brow.

“Best I not tell you,” Dulce said, stroking Swan like Dr. Evil. “Give you some plausible deniability in case the cops ever come calling.”

“They better not,” Rio said, dryly. “I didn’t bust my ass for you to become some Orange is the New Black extra.”

Dulce scoffed. “They can’t prove anything,” she said, standing up and walking, with Swan in her arms, to the door. “Also, you know I would be a main character, one with a tragic backstory.” That was the truth. “Anyway, I’m gonna go pass out for a few hours until my shift starts. Let me know if you need something, but also please don’t need anything.”

“You have work today?” Rio asked, removing Reaper from her shoulder, much to the cat’s chagrin. “After staying up all night?”

“I work in a coffee shop, Rio,” Dulce retorted. “I’m sure I’ll manage.”

Rio chuckled and rolled her eyes, plopping Reaper back onto her pillow and getting up to change for her walk to the park. Maybe, if she were lucky, Agatha would still show up for her jog.

People jogged in the rain, right?

 

Agatha did , in fact, show up on her run. Rio was sitting on her bench in a lime green rain poncho with a hood, sipping green tea from Dulce’s cafe. It had already gone mostly cold by the time Agatha finally came around the bend, but Rio didn’t mind. Her heart flipped when she saw Agatha in a purple windbreaker with the hood pulled up over her hair and a tiny backpack on her back.

“Fancy seeing you here,” she said, jogging in place as she stopped in front of Rio. “Figured you’d let Mother Nature handle the watering for the day.”

Was that an artist thing? Invoking the name of Mother Nature? It was cute, but creepy how similar she and Dulce were. Rio didn’t want to think about it. She was trying not to shudder as it was.

“Well, she can sprinkle a little rain,” Rio huffed, “but can she tell them how pretty they look and how proud she is of them?” Her voice devolved into a cartoonish baby voice as she turned to her azalea bush, stroking their petals, which were soft with raindrops. The bush itself was thriving.

“I hope somebody loves me as much as you love that singular azalea bush,” Agatha said. 

When Rio turned back to her with wide eyes, she was blushing and her lips were pressed tightly together like she hadn’t meant to say that aloud.

“I don’t think anybody will ever love anything the way I love Iggy,” Rio said. “She’s my baby.”

“Iggy?” Agatha asked.

“It’s what I named the bush,” Rio replied, keeping a straight face. That was not exactly a lie. Dulce had actually named the bush. But it was so clever, Rio perpetually took credit.

What Dulce didn’t know couldn’t hurt her.

Agatha snorted under her breath. “Of course you name your plants,” she said, almost inaudibly.

“You don’t?”

“I can’t keep anything alive long enough to name it,” Agatha admitted. “I’m much better with inanimate objects, but I’m not about to name my toaster, you know?”

“A toaster’s name would obviously be Toasty,” Rio said, without an ounce of humor evident on her face. It was a Herculean effort, truly.

“Is that what you named your toaster?” Agatha asked.

“Nah, mine is named Kevin.”

That got a startled laugh from Agatha’s lips, but a laugh nonetheless. Score one for Rio.

“Why Kevin?” Agatha asked.

“He’s red,” Rio said, shrugging. “Kinda reminded me of Kevin Bacon.”

“Why…?”

“Don’t try to understand my strange, lesion-riddled brain,” Rio said. “Even before the lesions, I was a pretty weird kid.”

“That doesn’t surprise me, actually,” Agatha said. “I’m betting you were the class clown?”

“I was a jock, actually,” Rio said, noting the look of surprise on Agatha’s face. “I played basketball all throughout high school and college while raising my little sister and working a part-time job at the local ice cream parlor.”

“Whoa,” Agatha said, her jogging in place slowing to a stop. “That sounds…rough. When did you sleep?”

“I blinked a lot.”

Agatha let out another snort at that.

Score two for Rio.

“Really, it was tough, but I got through it. My sister is practically a baby genius, anyway, so it wasn’t tough. Also, she was a total chick magnet. Girls used to tell me how cute she was and how much we looked alike.” She preened a little bit, then her shoulders slumped. “They only ever bought her milkshakes though.”

“Aww,” Agatha laughed. “That must have been so hard for you.”

“It was, yes, thank you,” Rio said. “At least she shared them with me.”

“Aww, that’s nice,” Agatha said. “Your sister sounds like a lot of fun. Is she single?”

“She’s straight,” Rio said, probably too quickly.

She actually didn’t know Dulce’s sexuality explicitly . She knew that she had dated one singular guy when she was way too young to have dated anybody, let alone somebody three years older than her when she was practically still pre-pubescent. If she ever saw that slimy Walker kid’s face again, he was going to quickly become fertilizer for her next azalea bush.

She didn’t know about anybody Dulce might have connected with in college, though. She’d always figured that Dulce was still too young for your average-aged college student, anyway. Rio had worried about her, of course, but Dulce was back every single weekend and school break, without fail.

Rio wondered if Dulce would even bother with the Pride celebrations they went to every June if it weren’t for the presence of her lesbian older sister.

“Darn,” Agatha said, snapping her fingers. “Well there goes that idea.” Her smile was teasing, though, and a little bit knowing.

Score one for Agatha, Rio thought to herself.

“So,” Rio said, desperate to change the subject, “how was your run?”

Agatha definitely noticed the change, but she didn’t comment on it as she looked at her watch again. “It’s not over quite yet,” she said. “I still have another half-mile to go before I reach my goal.”

“What’s your goal?” Rio asked. 

“Five miles,” Agatha said. 

Rio let out a low whistle. “That seems like a lot,” she said. “Does this town even have five miles to run or do you have to make a loop around Eastview, as well?”

Agatha snorted, shaking her head.

Half a point more for Rio.

“I run the paths in the park,” she said. “There’s enough of them for me to usually reach my goal. And I’m not opposed to repeating paths.” She shrugged.

“Where do you get the energy?” Rio asked.

“I am fueled by spite, typically,” Agatha said. 

Rio laughed. 

Damn. One more point for Agatha.

She was catching up.

“Well, do you have room for some coffee, too?” Rio asked. She held up her cup. “This went cold a while ago.”

“How long have you been sitting here?” Agatha asked, concern etched in her gaze.

“Since eight,” Rio said, shrugging. “Not that long. It’s only…” she looked at her phone screen, “9:07. Huh.”

“Let me finish this last half-mile,” Agatha said, with a lopsided smirk, “and I’ll buy you something hot at the cafe next-door to my gallery.”

“Sounds like a d–deal,” Rio stuttered, catching herself just in time. “I’ll be right here, cradling my ice cold tea.”

“I’ll be quick,” Agatha said before racing off. Rio watched her go, trying to keep her gaze respectful – and failing.

“What are the chances that I fuck this up before it even begins?” Rio asked the azalea bush. 

The silent response felt judgemental.

 

To her credit, running the last half-mile around the park took less than five minutes, and Agatha didn’t even look sweaty when she landed back in front of Rio, still energized as she ran in place. Rio’s eyes shone with amusement as she pushed herself up from the bench and followed the runner down the trail and out of the park, her poncho brushing Agatha’s windbreaker, but not other parts of their bodies brushing – which actually did take some effort considering how much she really wanted to reach for Agatha’s hand.

Rio tossed the remnants of her tea into a trash can on the corner just before they reached the cafe nestled right between a hardware store and Agatha’s gallery on the opposite corner. 

The cafe was called Witch’s Brew . Rio and Dulce had gone there nearly every time they visited the gallery together. Hell, she’d gone there just the other day when she attempted to see Agatha at the gallery. They knew her name by now.

“Good morning, Miss Rio,” the girl at the front, whose name tag read Kamala , greeted with a wide grin. “You’re here earlier than usual!” 

“You’re a regular?” Agatha asked, a bit of a teasing lilt in her voice.

“My sister and I come through on occasion,” Rio replied, blushing slightly.

“And good morning to you, too, Miss Agatha,” Kamala added. “Can I get you each your regular? Earl Grey, sweetened, with a pan au chocolat,” she pointed to Rio, “and an oat milk latte with two pumps of vanilla and a butter croissant, right?” She was looking at Agatha now.

“Yes, thank you, sweetheart,” Agatha said, unzipping the pocket of her windbreaker.

Rio beat her to it, however, pulling out her phone and pressing it to the POS, grinning in triumph when Agatha frowned at her, her credit card held uselessly in her hand.

“Hey,” she huffed, “I was gonna pay.”

“You can pay next time,” Rio said, her ears burning when she realized the implication of what she’d said. Agatha’s pleased smile kept her from clarifying, however, and she just let the words hang in the air as Kamala printed out her receipt and handed it over before she got started on making their drinks and warming their pastries. 

“You get us a table,” Agatha told Rio. “I’ll get the food.”

“I’m perfectly capable of carrying food, Agatha,” Rio huffed, “even with my limp.”

“You paid,” Agatha pointed out. “Let me at least do something and carry them to the table.”

Rio narrowed her eyes for a moment, trying to discern if Agatha was being genuine. It so often happened that people thought Rio incapable or even weak because of her cane that it was hard to tell if, right in this very moment, Agatha was being genuine and nonjudgmental of her disability. 

Agatha had one eyebrow raised in challenge, her arms crossed over her chest, no sympathy in her eyes. Rio decided to give her the benefit of the doubt and rolled her eyes.

“Fine,” she said, turning toward the tables. “Wow, it is so crowded in here,” she deadpanned, looking at the nearly empty cafe, “how ever will I find us a good place to sit?”

“I’m sure you’ll manage,” Agatha chuckled as she walked to the end of the bar where Kamala had already set the first pastry bag and a steaming cup of tea. 

Rio hobbled over to a four-top booth by the wide cafe window, slipping her slick poncho off and turning it inside out as she hung it on one of the hooks on the pillar next to the booth, then slipping into a seat, placing her cane next to her. She looked outside, where rain still fell – albeit a little more gently – and people strolled by with their own ponchos and trench coats and umbrellas, scurrying to work and wherever else somebody might go on a rainy Friday morning.

Usually, on a Friday morning, she’d be working on new material for her performance at The Witches’ Road that night. At this time, however, she’d more likely be sleeping, dead to the world until she dragged herself out of bed at noon to give herself and her cats something to eat for lunch before setting up her laptop at the kitchen table for the rest of the afternoon.

As Agatha placed down her steaming tea and warmed chocolate croissant, however, Rio decided that she’d much rather be here.

Agatha slid into the booth across from her and slipped off the windbreaker, balling the jacket up and dumping it unceremoniously onto the seat next to her with her backpack before undoing her ponytail and running her fingers through her curly long hair.

Rio malfunctioned at that exact moment, watching Agatha’s lithe fingers tangle in the dark brown locks, combing out knots and doing their best to fix the shape that the ponytail had locked her hair in for who knows how long. Rio’s own fingers twitched around her cup and she used them to bring the tea to her lips for a sip instead of doing what she wanted to do (reach out to run her own fingers through Agatha’s hair) instead. 

“So,” Agatha said, after finally fixing her hair so that most of the tangles were out and it hung over one shoulder, “tell me about your sister.”

“My sister?” Rio asked, tilting her head. “What about her?”

Agatha shrugged, her cheeks turning pink as she took another sip of her coffee. “Honestly,” she said, “I have no idea. You’ve mentioned her a few times and I really didn’t know how else to break the ice.” She looked down at her pastry bag, pulling out her warmed croissant. It was steaming slightly. Agatha tore a small piece off and popped it into her mouth with a pleased hum, licking some crumbs off of her lips. 

Rio’s throat went dry at the sight, her cheeks warming. She took a sip of her own drink and cleared her throat.

“Um, she’s good,” she said. “She’s…she’s a super genius, honestly. She was accelerated through school when she was little and ended up graduating college at eighteen.”

Agatha’s eyes widened at that. “Seriously?” she asked. “Whoa.”

Rio smiled, somewhat proudly. “Yeah, she’s…kind of incredible. Still a snarky little shit, though. She gets that from me.”

“Your parents must be proud,” Agatha commented, taking another bite of the croissant.

Rio tried not to let her face fall at the mention of her parents. It’s not like Agatha knew about the difficult relationship they had, the therapy that she’d put Dulce and herself through to get over abandonment issues that neither of them were even aware that they’d had the first year after they moved out of their house. 

They hadn’t even heard from their parents for a few years now, not since Dulce graduated from undergrad at eighteen and they were let off the hook for child support.

“I was never really your mother,” Magda Vidal had told Dulce at the dinner she and their father had treated them to after her ceremony. “I gave birth to you, but Rio was the one who raised you.”

“I didn’t have to be,” Rio had pointed out, not at all bitter about it. She squeezed Dulce’s hand under the table to reassure her. “I could have just been her sister if you let me be.”

“You already know that we never wanted children,” their father, Luis, said. It was said as a matter of fact, no emotion. “We were too young the first time, with Rio, and then we were overexcited and underprepared the second time, with Dulcita.”

“Don’t call me that,” Dulce said. “That’s not my name.”

Luis’s eyes widened in surprise, but he nodded. “ Lo siento ,” he said. “We were never the parents you deserved,” he admitted. “We should have been. But we just…we couldn’t. Now, it’s too late.”

Rio’s jaw clenched. She didn’t agree with that at all. She was thirty years old and she had already raised a child from infancy to legal adulthood, but in some ways she felt as though they still both needed their parents.

But she wasn’t about to beg for them.

So she just nodded, thanked them for paying for the meal, and waved to their car after it pulled away from the curb.

That was five years ago. They never even got Happy Birthday texts after that. So they never sent them, either.

Who needed them?

Rio must have been quiet for too long, lost in thought, because she noticed Agatha’s smile starting to drop as her eyebrows knotted together. She opened her mouth to say something else, but Rio beat her to it.

“They’re not really around anymore,” Rio admitted, trying to stay as vague as possible while remaining casual.

“Oh,” Agatha said. She cleared her throat. “Sorry.”

“Don’t be,” Rio insisted. “It’s fine. We never really needed them, anyway. I raised Dulce all by myself.”

“Dulce?” Agatha echoed.

“My sister,” Rio explained. “She was named after my mother’s pregnancy cravings. A lot of chocolate.”

Agatha smiled at that, but there was something else in her eyes – something like recognition. “Is that a Vidal trait?” she asked. “A love of chocolate.”

“It’s in our blood,” Rio said, taking a bite of her croissant. “Probably from our deep Puerto Rican roots.” She licked a little bit of chocolate she could feel from her lips and watched, in poorly-disguised satisfaction, at the way Agatha’s eyes tracked the movement. “Chocolate has always been big in Latin America, you know?”

“Yeah…” Agatha said, looking very distracted still as Rio wiped some chocolate from the corner of her mouth with her thumb and then stuck her thumb into her mouth, fighting for her life not to smile as those blue eyes seemed to darken slightly.

If she had any doubt before that Agatha Harkness was into her, it was gone now.

Rio just didn’t know how to go about officially asking her out on a date…

Instead, she continued to talk about the history of chocolate, how the Spaniards had actually brought it over during their colonization in the late 16th or early 17th centuries. She was basically just echoing a lot of facts she’d learned for a grad school paper about the cacao plant, but she found it interesting how it was loosely tied to her roots. 

“What we know as modern chocolate originated with the Aztecs,” she said, tearing apart her croissant, “but it was actually the Olmecs who started using cacao in a traditional bitter drink. Aztecs gave chocolate its name, derived from the Aztec word xocoatl. ”

“Wow,” Agatha said, her cheeks flushed for some odd reason, “you sure know a lot about the history of chocolate.”

“I’m very passionate about sweet things,” Rio replied, popping another piece of her croissant into her mouth and making sure she shared intense eye contact with Agatha, trying to convey something she couldn’t yet say with words. That blush on Agatha’s cheeks deepened, and she was the first to break eye contact as she took another sip of her latte, clearing her throat. Rio chuckled under her breath as she sipped her tea.

They sipped and snacked for a long moment before Rio cleared her throat.

“So,” she said, “do you have any siblings? Besides Jen, I mean.”

Agatha snorted and shook her head. “Only child,” she said, “thankfully.”

“Aw, you don’t want somebody who steals your most comfy clothes or throws fake spiders at you?” Rio teased.

“I don’t want somebody to have the same trauma as me,” Agatha said before gasping and placing her hand over her mouth and looking away.

Rio pressed her lips together and took a deep breath, nodding. “That’s fair,” she said. “I wish I could have protected Dulce against that better.” She tapped her fingers on her cup. “Were they terrible?”

“ She was, yes,” Agatha admitted. “But my grandmother took me away to live with her when I was still little. And she was…” Agatha gave Rio a small, serene smile, “the best.”

“Can you tell me about her?” Rio asked. At the curious look Agatha gave her, Rio shrugged. “My mom’s parents disowned her for having a baby out of wedlock. My dad’s mom died when I was a toddler, and he never knew his dad. I didn’t grow up with grandparents, and, at twelve, I more or less became a parent myself.”

Agatha smiled and nodded. “Her name was Isla,” she said. “She was my favorite person in the world.”

Rio listened with rapt attention as Agatha told her about her grandmother, laughing and tearing up a little (but never fully allowing herself to cry at the same time) as she told little anecdotes of her childhood growing up under her gran’s care, how her grandmother supported her with her art and anything else she put her mind to, how she had passed before Agatha finished college. Rio reached out, initiating physical contact between them for the first time, placing her hand over Agatha’s in the center of the table.

Agatha thankfully didn’t pull away.

By the time their pastries were gone and the last dregs of their hot drinks had gone cold, the rain had stopped pouring outside and Kamala was standing awkwardly at the side of their table with a sheepish smile on her face.

“Hey, ladies,” she said, “I’m so sorry to have to do this, but my manager says that there’s a one-hour time limit to sit here and you two passed that about fifteen minutes ago. He’s getting antsy.” She glanced behind her to where a graying old man stood behind the counter with a sourpuss expression. Kamala gave them an apologetic smile.

Rio looked at her phone. It was almost eleven.

“It’s fine, sweetie,” Agatha said with a laugh. “We should actually get going, anyway.” She pulled her hand away from Rio’s and reached instead for her jacket and backpack. 

While Rio mourned the loss of her hand, she did follow Agatha in preparing to leave, swiping the crumbs from the table into the pastry bag and then tossing that bag and her empty cup into a nearby trash can. She offered Kamala a soft smile as she slipped back into her now-dry poncho, leaving the hood down as she waited for Agatha to zip up her windbreaker and put her backpack back on.

When they were ready to go, Agatha motioned for Rio to follow her but unfortunately didn’t reach for her hand again, much to Rio’s disappointment. Still, Rio followed her (at this point, she thought, she might follow Agatha into an active volcano.)

It was a short walk to the front door of the gallery, where people were already milling about, looking at the art on the walls behind the large reception desk. There was a college-aged girl at the front, selling tickets and answering a phone. When she saw Agatha, she gave her a wide grin and a big wave.

“Hi, Miss Harkness!” she called out.

“Hello, America,” Agatha replied, smiling back. 

She led Rio toward one of a set of doors to the left of the entrance. 

“This is my office,” she said. “Just give me a moment to get changed.” She motioned toward the ensuite bathroom. “I mean, you can obviously go and start looking around. I’ll catch up.”

“I can wait,” Rio said, sitting down on a sofa that was against the wall adjacent to the door. “I need to sit for a minute, anyway.”

“I’ll be quick,” Agatha promised, heading toward the door. 

“Take your time,” Rio said, settling against one of the pillows on the couch. It was ridiculously comfy. She could probably fall asleep here. She wondered if Agatha had ever taken a cat nap in the middle of a work day.

“Alright,” Agatha laughed. “Be right back.”

She shut the bathroom door with a decisive click and Rio leaned back, her gaze roaming around the room. 

The room itself was decently-sized, with a desk, some bookshelves, the couch she was sitting on, a deep purple area rug, and a few pieces of art on the walls. Good art, of course. Paintings that obviously took some skill to make. Rio wondered for a moment if Agatha had made them, but then she saw the tiny paper plaques next to them, labeling them as the work of other artists. 

One was a piece by Jen Kale, a piece of a beach house with a bright blue sky and a pier in the distance. It looked so real that Rio could almost feel the sea air, could almost hear the waves against the shore, smell the ocean…

Damn, Jen actually seemed to have talent.

Good thing she was just Agatha’s sister, huh?

She’s embarrassed to say that she was relieved to learn that Agatha’s ‘partner’ wasn’t in the romantic sense. She felt as though Agatha had been trying to hint at that, in retrospect, but Rio had been entirely clueless about it until she made it clear by taking Jen Kale with her to their group the previous night.

And now said sister was about to go on a date with Rio’s best friend.

It was like a frickin’ romantic comedy. With lesbians.

(Which, in Rio’s humble opinion, was how all rom-coms should be.)

She wandered around the room, looking at the art, her eyes roaming over every inch until she caught something along the top edge of the wall. Her eyes inched upward and her jaw dropped the second she saw the stars.

When Agatha emerged from the bathroom, Rio was lying in the middle of her office floor, staring up at the ceiling, her cane clutched in her hand at her side.

“Uh…whatcha doin’ there?” Agatha asked, padding to the other woman to loom over her, her hair in two long, loose braids falling over her shoulders. 

Rio grinned up at her, somewhat sheepishly. She looked pretty like that.

“Uh, I was looking at the stars?” she offered. “This is the easiest way to see everything.” She motioned to the ceiling, where an entire solar system, complete with realistic constellations, nebulas, and planets had been painted on the ceiling, centered around the round globe light, which was acting as the Sun. It must have taken ages to paint all of that.

Agatha grinned down at her and held up one finger, walking over to the door. There was a singular window on it so that one could see into the office. Rio watched, upside down, as Agatha pulled down the tiny shade on the door, then pressed the sides to some velcro strips on the sides and bottom, removing any light leakage from outside the office, then reached for the light switch right next to the door.

Rio blinked into the sudden darkness, her eyes returning to the ceiling as her jaw fell open. The painted stars and galaxies above her head began to glow in a rainbow of iridescent color. The planets had dark backgrounds with their textures painted on them in glowing lines. The nebulas were like shimmering clouds. The stars stood out the most, painting the familiar constellations all over the ceiling and spilling onto the top few inches of the walls.

“Whoa…” Rio said, her eyes taking in everything with new appreciation. “This is…wow. Did you do this all yourself?”

“No,” Agatha said, walking over to plop herself onto the floor next to Rio, lying down a couple inches away. Their fingers brushed momentarily before she settled and Rio felt her cheeks go hot. “I had help,” Agatha continued. “Jen painted most of the planets, using some kind of map for ‘the best alignment’ so I didn’t perpetually have my Mercury in retrograde or whatever.”

“That would require an actual mobile Mercury,” Rio pointed out. “Retrograde just means that it appears to be moving away from the Earth.”

“Whatever, nerd,” Agatha teased. “She said something like that. I just let her do whatever she wanted with them. A few of the planets are painted with her own customized paints. I did the glow lines over them, though.”

“They look really cool,” Rio said. “Are you into astronomy?”

“Astrology, actually,” Agatha said. “Gran was half-Greek. Her Yia-Yia used to tell her all the Greek myths from this big book at bedtime. She, in turn, told them to my dad, who was just starting to tell them to me when he passed.”

Rio’s smile fell and she found herself immediately reaching for Agatha’s hand. She hadn’t said anything about her dad when they were sitting at the cafe earlier. For some reason, she had just assumed that Gran was Agatha’s mother’s mom, though how somebody so sweet could have raised anybody that cruel was beyond her. 

Now, at least, it made sense.

“I’m okay,” Agatha said, still allowing Rio to squeeze her hand. “I was two, he didn’t live with us, and I honestly don’t have many memories of him. Most of what I know was told to me by my gran, and she’s incredibly biased.”

“Still,” Rio murmured, “it must be tough. Was he nice, at least?”

“From what gran told me and from what I remember? Yeah,” Agatha said. Rio could hear the strain in her voice. “He was nice. He used to bring me to my gran’s for breakfast, and he insisted on cutting up my strawberries himself.” Agatha smiled softly. “He fashioned the pieces into pictures for me. Gran said that’s where I get my artistic talent – cut-up strawberries.” She laughed and Rio joined her. “My only memory of him is sitting on his shoulders while we walk through the zoo. I don’t even remember which zoo we were at, but there were elephants. And there was a giraffe that stooped over the fence to look me right in the eye. I got to feed it something. He bought me a stuffed one, I think, but I have no idea where it is now. Mother probably tossed it as soon as I got home.”

“Fucking bitch,” Rio grumbled, tightening her hold on Agatha’s hand. “Full offense to her.”

“Deserved,” Agatha laughed. “I go to his grave on Father’s Day. I bring azaleas because those were gran’s favorite flowers, and I figure they might have also been his.”

“What’s his name?” Rio asked, her voice barely above a whisper as they continued to look up at the ceiling.

“Nicholas,” Agatha whispered back. 

“Good name,” Rio murmured.

“I’m going to name my son that,” Agatha said. “You know, if I ever have one.” Rio glanced toward her to see a blush appear on her cheeks in the low glow of the painted stars above. 

She could see Agatha as a mother, with a little boy with her blue eyes and curly brown hair and big smile and beautiful nose…and a little gap between his teeth, maybe…

That’s not how that works, dumbass .

Rio snapped her eyes back to the ceiling, swallowing thickly as she continued to scan the painted night sky.

“You’d make a good mom,” she said, after a long moment.

Agatha chuckled at that. “You barely know me,” she said. “I could be a shitty mom. I could continue the cycle of abuse.”

“You wouldn’t,” Rio said, with certainty. “Not after what your mother did to you. Besides, you got out of there. The woman who actually raised you sounds pretty cool. You could follow her example.”

“You think?”

Rio felt Agatha’s breath hit her cheek and turned her head to face the other woman, finding Agatha’s bright blue eyes staring back at her. Suddenly, her mouth was very dry and she was fighting for her life not to let her gaze flicker down to Agatha’s lips, which were full and plump and dammit she was looking, wasn’t she?

Agatha seemed to be having a similar problem keeping her gaze above Rio’s nose-level.

“Definitely,” Rio said, a little too delayed. She swallowed. “You would be a great mother, Agatha Harkness. There’s no doubt in my mind.”

Agatha didn’t say anything to that. She just nibbled at her bottom lip. Her gaze flickered back down to Rio’s lips, and Rio’s breath stuttered in her lungs.

Without fully making the decision to, Rio found herself leaning toward Agatha. To her surprise and delight, Agatha was also moving toward her, their hands still clutching each other, fingers slotted together as their noses brushed and–

“Why is the light off?”

Agatha sprang away from her, tearing her hand from Rio’s so quickly that her fingers ached. She jumped to her feet as the lights came on above their heads, spinning around to face Jen Kale, who had entered the office without so much as a knock .

Rio was back to disliking the other woman. At least this time she had both met her and found a legitimate reason to feel this way.

Rio attempted to get up as quickly, but her lower leg was on pins and needles and her cane would just not cooperate, so it ended up with her turtling on the floor for about a minute while the other two women watched, staring down at her blankly.

“You know,” Rio said, “you could help.”

“Right,” Agatha said, reaching down to hook her arm around Rio’s, giving her the support she needed to get to her feet, planting her cane firmly on the ground to help her stay upright. “You okay?” Agatha asked.

“Yeah, I’m fi-ine,” Rio said, her breath stuttering when she turned her head to find that she and Agatha were back in their previous position, noses brushing as they stared into each other’s eyes.

Jen cleared her throat after about five seconds of this, and Rio found her dislike quickly turning to actual hatred of the other woman as Agatha stepped away with a pathetic little pat-pat on Rio’s shoulder.

Then she turned to Jen with an annoyed expression (good).

“Have you forgotten how to knock, Jennifer?” she asked, an edge to her tone as she crossed her arms and cocked her hip.

“Have you forgotten that we had a meeting scheduled in my office about fifteen minutes ago?” Jen Kale retorted. “You’re lucky I gave you a grace period, Harkness.”

“What meeting?” Agatha huffed, slipping her phone out of her pocket and swiping through it. She let out a groan. “Ugh, I completely forgot.” Agatha offered Rio an apologetic smile. “I’m so sorry about this,” she said, “but, unfortunately , my annoying co-worker–”

“ Business partner ,” Jen corrected.

“- is correct. We have a meeting.” She glared at Jen, who was smiling triumphantly. “I hate you.”

“You love me,” Jen snorted. She looked at Rio. “It’s nice to see you again, Rio,” she said. “I’m sorry for interrupting, but I honestly expected Agatha to be in the office like an hour ago.”

Rio tried to play it casual, like Agatha’s pseudo-sister/business partner/resident annoyance didn’t just interrupt what might have been… something . 

“No worries,” Rio said, shrugging. Chill, casual, not blue-balled (lipped?) at all. “Work comes first.”

“Um,” Agatha said, “it should only take about twenty minutes, if you wanna wait for me. In the gallery, of course. You shouldn’t be cooped up in my office that entire time. I can come meet you afterward,” she cleared her throat, “if you want.”

Suddenly, Agatha Harkness was shy, her entire face red and a little bit of sweat on her brow as she stuck her hands into the pockets of a pair of extremely well-fitted indigo trousers. And Rio was fucking charmed by it. Damn her.

“Sure,” she said. “Text me when you’re done and I’ll let you know where I’m at.”

“Well,” Agatha said, chuckling a little, “I would need your phone number to do that.”

“Right,” Rio said, feeling her own cheeks warm up. “Um…may I?” She pointed to Agatha’s phone. Agatha nodded, swiping on it a few times before handing it over. 

Rio entered her number and then took a second longer than necessary to come up with a name for herself.

She settled on “Rio💀🌺”, sent herself “wazzuuuuup?” and handed the phone back.

When Agatha saw what she’d done, she snorted under her breath. “You’re such a geek,” she teased, slipping her phone back in her pocket. “I’ll text you in a bit. Let me walk you over to America so she can give you a wristband.” She looked at Jen. “Go to your office. I’ll be right there,” she promised, waving Jen away with her hand.

Rio felt some stupid satisfaction at that as she followed Agatha out to the reception desk, where they immediately skipped the line.

A few people complained, but Agatha ignored them, flashing an ID badge, with the word “Owner” written clearly in big block letters, she’d pulled from her pocket, still not even glancing at them as she smiled at the girl behind the counter.

“Hello, America, dear,” she said, her voice honey-sweet. “Can you get my friend, Miss Vidal here,” she placed her hand on Rio’s shoulder, which sent shivers down her gay little spine, “a wristband?”

“Of course, Miss Harkness,” the girl, America, said, reaching under the counter and pulling out a wristband. Rio stuck out her left wrist, and the girl wrapped the band around her wrist, keeping it just loose enough so that it wouldn’t be uncomfortable, but tight enough that it wouldn’t slip off as she walked around. “When you’re done,” the girl said, “please discard your wristband in the recycling vestibule next to the entrance so that it doesn’t end up in a landfill somewhere. You can do your part to save the planet.”

“Naturally,” Rio agreed with a nod and a smirk. “Thanks, America.”

“Anything for a friend of Miss Harkness,” America chirped happily.

“For future reference, Miss Vidal doesn’t pay the entrance fee when she comes in,” Agatha said, lowering her voice so that the people in line behind them didn’t hear. “Put her on the List .”

America nodded, clicking a few things on her computer and typing something in before giving Agatha a thumbs up. 

“Her sister, too,” Agatha said, almost as an afterthought. “Dulce Vidal.”

“Oh,” America said, typing the first few letters of Dulce’s name, “she’s already on the list.”

“What?” Agatha asked, her eyes widening as she stepped around to stand behind America, looking at the monitor over her shoulder.

“She’s a contributor,” America informed her. “She gets free access to the gallery for a year. We’re selling prints of her work in the shop.”

Agatha blinked at the screen, then her gaze went to Rio, who was very confused by the look the other woman was giving her. It was almost…dazed, like she was shocked to learn this information. 

Did she not know who Dulce was? Dulce had said that Agatha spoke to her directly and personally on the night of her showcase, right here in this very gallery. Why did she look so taken aback?

Agatha seemed to snap out of it, schooling her features as she offered America another gentle smile. “Of course,” she said. “Completely slipped my mind. Thank you, dear.” She patted America on the shoulder then turned back to Rio. “I’ll see you in a bit,” she said, almost professionally detached. “Enjoy the artwork.”

“Um,” Rio said, as Agatha started back toward the offices, “thanks.”

She was thoroughly confused.

Chapter End Notes

Chapter 8: Bottom Behavior

Chapter Summary

Agatha threatens Jen with bodily harm and Jen retaliates by calling her a Bottom.

Chapter Notes

Happy Disability Pride Month! I tried really hard to get this out on the first, but my ADHD was like "no" and there are so many distractions, so I'm going to make an effort to post at least once a week for this story. At LEAST.

I'm so happy that so many people seem to like this and I hope you continue to like it as we get deeper into the story. Leave comments to let me know. Enjoy!

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Chapter Eight

“I am going to murder you.”

“Don’t be so dramatic,” Jen huffed, sitting at her desk with her laptop and a pile of papers in front of her. “You can go back to making out with the cute comedian as soon as you’ve signed all these papers.” She placed her hand on a stack of paper a foot high. 

“What’s all this for?” Agatha asked. “And we weren’t making out .”

They didn’t even get to kiss . Though they were pretty close…before Jen came in and ruined it.

She would make it a slow, painful death while also making it look like an accident. She wondered if sprinkling a little bleach on her tofu would do it. It could take weeks of agony…

“Then why were you lying on the floor?” Jen asked, raising one eyebrow as Agatha took one of the seats on the other side of her desk and reached for the pile of paper, which was littered with colorful tabs instructing her to ‘Sign’ here or ‘Initial’ there. She reached for the cup of pens and made sure to pick the most expensive-looking one, totally planning on slipping it into her pocket by accident. She knew it would drive Jen crazy.

“Do you usually have makeout sessions on the floor, Jen?” Agatha huffed. “Didn’t know you got down like that? Does Alice know?”

“Stop deflecting,” Jen huffed. “And just tell me.”

“No, we weren’t making out,” Agatha said, her eyes skimming through the first release form. “We were…looking at the stars.”

She felt her cheeks go hot as she avoided Jen’s gaze, trying to focus on the words swimming on the page. Unfortunately, she’d left her reading glasses in the drawer of her desk. Great.

She just signed her name where she saw the pink tab, right below where Jen had already signed hers. They said the same thing every time, anyway.

Jen was handling the next showcase for the gallery. It was for a group of artists that painted scenes from all around the world, from Egypt to Italy, to Finland, to Brazil, and everywhere in between. They had a total of eighteen paintings between them. Each one required its own release form in order to be put in the showcase. After Agatha and Jen signed all of them, the artists themselves would get copies and start moving their work into the backroom of the gallery so that they could make prints to sell in their gift shop.

The working title for the showcase was ‘Around the World in Eighteen Paintings’ which Agatha thought was pretty on the nose and lame, but she honestly couldn’t think of a better name for it and the promotional material had to go out that weekend on social media or turnout was likely to be very low.

Their gallery survived on showcases, for the most part. Ticket sales for everyday patrons weren’t as high as they could have been, in Agatha’s opinion.

Not that she was really even thinking about that now as she signed form after form, initialing here and there, before setting each one aside. The letters didn’t really look like anything to her – just formless squiggles. Her mind was elsewhere as she signed them.

“Agatha?” Jen snapped her fingers in front of her as Agatha reached for the next form. Her fingers found the flat, bare surface of Jen’s desk instead. 

There was nothing left.

“You okay?” Jen asked, looking genuinely concerned as Agatha blinked up at her. “You look…dazed.” Then her lips turned up in a grin. “Thinking about Rio?” she sing-songed.

Agatha furrowed her brow and pursed her lips together. She wanted to deny it, but even if she was good at lying, she didn’t think she’d be any bit convincing at this moment. Because the honest truth was yes , she was certainly thinking about Rio right now.

More specifically, though, she was thinking about Rio’s sister, Dulce. Dulce Vidal. Dulce Vidal, who Rio had pretty much raised on her own while somehow still being a semi-normal teenager with hobbies and a part-time job.

Dulce Vidal, who was a prodigy, a genius who had graduated early from high school and college. Dulce Vidal, who was a talented photographer, whose work had been featured in their last showcase at the gallery. Whose work Agatha had bid on and even won and brought home to hang in her studio.

Dulce Vidal, Rio’s sister, who had used her sister as the subject for her featured photograph. Agatha couldn’t believe she hadn’t seen it earlier: the figure in the photograph was Rio , with her cane, standing on a stage with the lights casting her almost entirely in shadow.

Agatha wanted nothing more than to rush home to look at that photograph now, to stare at it and try to see all the features she’d missed previously, the ones that would point to Rio being the obvious subject of the piece. She wanted to see if she could make out her features in the shadows cast over her face. Would she be able to see Rio’s smile? That cute little gap between her teeth? The mischief in her eyes?

Or would the confidence in her stance give away her identity, now that Agatha knew to look for it? Would she recognize the unique shape of Rio’s custom cane this time? Would she notice the tears in the knees of her jeans, also shrouded in shadow? Or the green converse that Rio wore to the support group and the park the last time they met? Would all of these parts have come together eventually if Agatha never learned that Dulce was Rio’s sister?

Would she have never learned that? She supposed she would have, eventually, if things continued to progress the way that they were.

Which was how, exactly?

“Agatha,” Jen’s voice broke through her reverie and she looked up, once again blinking up at her business partner/sister. “Girl, are you okay?” Jen asked. “You look flushed.” 

She reached under her desk and came back with a small bottle of spring water, pressing it into Agatha’s hand. “Take a drink, please, before you pass out or something.”

Agatha nodded, unscrewing the cap on the bottle and taking a long sip. It was refreshing, she had to admit, after she’d jogged five miles and then drank only a coffee afterward. Jen had gotten onto her before about needing to drink more water after runs. She tried to remember to do that, but coffee mostly took priority as her drink of choice to replenish her energy.

Yes, she was dumb about it. She knew that.

She finished the water in a few gulps and Jen just handed her another.

“Where are you getting these?” Agatha asked between sips. “And how are they so cold?”

“I installed a mini-fridge underneath my desk,” Jen informed her. “Mainly for my yogurt, so I don’t have to get up to go get it and nobody steals it now.”

Agatha tried not to frown. She had been wondering why there was no more yogurt in the kitchen fridge.

“That’s smart,” she said. “Why do you have water in it?”

“Because you’re supposed to drink water every now and then, Agatha,” Jen said, giving her a look like ‘duh’ that made Agatha start planning to sneak in when Jen was out to steal her fancy Greek yogurt and replace it with a cheap off-brand version. “Now, what’s going on with you? You seem…disturbed by something.”

Agatha took a deep breath and slowly sipped at the last bits of water in the tiny bottle that Jen had given her, debating whether to tell her what she had just learned. It was ridiculous how tiny this town was. Would Jen just learn this eventually, anyway?

Did she already know?

“What do you know about the girl whose photograph I bought last weekend?” she asked, finally looking up at an impatient Jen.

Jen’s brows furrowed. “What?” she asked. “Um, not that much really.” She stood and walked over to one of her filing cabinets, opening a drawer and pulling out a file labeled ‘Davis Advanced Photography Showcase’. She flipped it open as she padded back (Agatha noticed for the first time that Jen had shed her shoes and was now walking barefoot across the pink fuzzy rug on her hardwood floor; her toes were freshly painted) and started thumbing through the forms. “Dulce, right?” she asked, looking up at Agatha as she sat back down in her chair.

Agatha nodded.

Jen pulled out a paperclipped file that had a print attached to it. It was a miniature version of the photograph she’d bought. Jen pulled the paperclip off and spread the papers over her desk. There was also a small picture of Dulce Vidal, in black and white, amongst the papers. She was smiling wide with dimpled cheeks and big, dark eyes, and Agatha couldn’t believe she hadn’t seen the similarities before.

They were obviously sisters.

“What about her?” Jen asked after a moment of just looking down at the papers spread out in front of her. “What am I missing?”

Agatha pointed a finger at the figure in Dulce’s photograph. “What do you see?”

“Uh…a person?” Jen said. “I dunno, Agatha. It’s the subject of Dulce’s photograph. What am I supposed to see?”

“Look closely,” Agatha instructed. “Who do you think that is?”

“Who…?” Jen looked down at the photograph, picking it up to get a closer look. Her eyes scanned the whole thing for a long moment, moving from corner to corner, methodically. 

Then they narrowed on something in the photograph.

“Holy shit,” she gasped under her breath. “Is this…?”

Agatha nodded. “I can’t believe I didn’t see it before.”

“How do they even know each other?” Jen asked, picking up the picture of Dulce and holding it up next to the photograph of Rio in shadow.

“They’re sisters,” Agatha murmured.

“You’re kidding! ” Jen gasped. “Holy shit.” Her gaze bounced between the two photographs, wide in shock as she took in all this new information.

And then she started laughing.

Not soft chuckles like “Oh wow, what a funny coincidence, huh?” but full-on belly laughs that got louder and more intense with every second until tears were spilling from her eyes and she was holding onto her sides as her shoulders shook. The photographs fluttered out of her hands, landing back onto the desk, just over Dulce’s release form for her art. “I’m sorry!” she snorted out at Agatha’s look of shock. “I’m s-sorry!” She laughed harder at the indignation in Agatha’s expression, unable to keep talking as all the breath in her lungs was taken out by her mirth.

“You are the worst ‘sister’ ever,” Agatha huffed, standing up. 

She turned to leave, but Jen’s hand around her wrist caught her, pulling her back.

“No, wait,” she said. “I’m sorry, I’m sorry.” She was still chuckling, trying desperately to clear the laughter from her throat as tears fell down her cheeks. “Oh god,” she said. “I’m so sorry, but I'm really not laughing at you. I’m just…this really is a miniscule town, huh? Like, what are the odds ?” She let out another giggle.

Agatha let out a deep sigh, her body sagging slightly as she slumped back down in the seat. “Apparently not as high as one would think,” she said. “I feel like I should have known earlier though.”

“How could you?” Jen asked, reaching for a tissue to wipe at her eyes, carefully avoiding her mascara and eyeliner. “I mean, yeah, Westview is ridiculously tiny, but you still don’t expect shit like this ,” she motioned to the pictures of Dulce and Rio, respectively, “to happen. It’s like some kind of…” she waved her hand around, trying to find the word.

“Greek tragedy?” Agatha asked, remembering the stories that her gran used to tell her and the book she’d given Agatha when she was thirteen years old, composed of Greek myths, plays, and other stories about idiots who ignored the all-knowing oracles and got themselves into Situations.

“I don’t think I’d call what you and Rio have a tragedy ,” Jen said, patting Agatha’s hand. “That’s a bit pessimistic, don’t you think?”

“I’m just going based off of my own history,” Agatha huffed. “Life hasn’t exactly been kind to me. I started life with a psychotic, hateful bitch of a mother, and ended up getting diagnosed with a degenerative neurological disease before my thirtieth birthday. What would you call that besides tragic?”

Jen shrugged. “Unlucky, for sure,” she said. “But getting diagnosed with MS led to you and Rio meeting in the first place, didn’t it?”

“We might have met, anyway,” Agatha pointed out. “Apparently she’s a regular at the cafe next door. And she and her sister like to come to the gallery quite often together.”

“So do a lot of people,” Jen pointed out. “If she’d just been a patron, would you have even considered asking her out.”

“I still haven’t,” Agatha pointed out. Jen scoffed. “I haven’t !”

“Maybe not officially,” Jen said, “but you can’t tell me that you two weren’t on a date when I walked into your office earlier. I mean, you were stargazing , Agatha.”

“They were fake stars!” Agatha protested. “Painted on my ceiling. I just came out of the bathroom and she was lying on my floor, looking at them. That doesn’t mean it was a date.”

“Did you go with her to get coffee?” Jen asked.

“Hmm?” Agatha replied, avoiding her gaze.

“You said she’s apparently a regular at the coffee shop next door,” Jen reminded her. “You must have found out about that somehow . Did you two go there together?”

“Yes,” Agatha said. “It was on our way and we were trying to get out of the rain.”

“Ten feet from the gallery?” Jen snorted. “Okay. Who paid?”

“What does that matter?” Agatha huffed, squirming in her seat.

“Well, if you paid separately, then it’s definitely not a date,” Jen said. “It’s just two women (lesbians, albeit) hanging out and having a drink. But if one of you paid for both drinks…”

Agatha took a deep breath, readying herself for what was about to come next.

“She paid,” she admitted.

“HA!” Jen said, getting to her feet and pointing directly in Agatha’s face. For now, at least.

Chapter 9: Stage Fright

Chapter Summary

Rio's show goes about as well as one might expect. Switching POVs.

Chapter Notes

I am not a comedian, which became glaringly obvious to me as I attempted to write a comedy set for this chapter. I apologize if it's unfunny, but at least I tried.

There IS some more Dulce/Rio lore for those of you have expressed your appreciation for that.

Also, shoutout to TremorDusk for helping me as best as they can (I may be a hopeless case but I appreciate you) to structure a standup routine and set up the stand up scene. I did my best.

Also, reminder that Dulce Vidal is played by Jenna Ortega in my head and so that's who you should picture when her parts come up in the story. Thanks.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Chapter Nine

Rio Vidal did not get stage fright.

She never had. Not since her first time at an open mic night at her school when she was seventeen years old, on one of the odd nights her parents remembered that they were, in fact, the parents of a five-year-old, not her. She had only gone to support one of her teammates, Ana, who was the class clown and wanted to try her hand at actually telling jokes in front of a crowd.

As it turned out, goofing off during class and being a smart-ass was way less nerve-wracking than doing an actual set like a real comedian. Ana had frozen on stage with the spotlight shining right in her face, which went almost impressively pale in a matter of seconds while people in the crowd waited for her to speak.

Rio had come to her rescue (only partially because she had the tiniest crush on Ana and her curly black hair and singular dimple in her left cheek) and shooed her off-stage as she began telling a funny story about how she mistook an essay prompt in her philosophy class and ended up writing a whole five-page paper on Youth in Asia.

“It was apparently good enough to bag me an A in Miss Stabile’s class,” she said to the roaring laughter of her classmates, “but now I’m required to go for extra lessons in spelling. If you ask me, Miss S should be required to take lessons in writing because you can’t see shit on that board.” There was more laughter and a few cheers of agreement about the young teacher’s atrocious penmanship.

Before she knew it, she’d completed a whole five-minute stand up routine before being shooed off the stage for the next scheduled act. She got a standing ovation as she stepped off the stage, receiving pats on the back and congratulations.

Ana had even pressed a kiss to her cheek, which was the highlight of young gay Rio’s life.

After that, she told jokes and did stand-up whenever she could, even when she had Dulce hanging off of her leg. Sometimes especially when Dulce was there because, somehow, her giggles made Rio’s joke that much funnier to the crowd.

So, yeah, Rio never had stage fright. If anything, she felt energized by a crowd. The more people out there, the better.

But that didn’t mean she didn’t get nervous when there were certain people in the crowd. Specifically, one person. 

One person with long, curly dark hair and eyes the shade of crystal…who isn’t even here yet.

Rio looked at the time on her phone. She had ten minutes before she had to go on. Most of the seats were already filled. Alice sat in the front row of folding chairs, next to three “Reserved” signs, looking at her own watch. Jen must have been running late, too.

Obviously, dumbass , the mean little voice in her head said. Does it look like she’s sitting there to you? Rio told the voice to fuck off and shut the curtain as she started to pace, clutching her cane with one hand and looking at the notes for her set with the other. She didn’t really ever need them because she had a pretty great memory and Dulce always held up notecards in the front if there was too long of a pause on stage, just to remind her which joke to go to next in case Rio ever did forget, but it was something to do, she reasoned, that could take her mind off the waiting. 

She hated waiting.

“Ooh, good crowd!”

Rio jumped at her sister’s voice and nearly toppled over as she turned to see Dulce standing there, already holding her phone up to film Rio, as she did nearly every Saturday.

“Say cheese!” she said, tilting her head so that Rio could see her big grin. She never failed to make Rio smile in return.

Tonight was no exception.

Rio gave the camera the finger.

“Very nice,” Dulce laughed. She put the phone down and slipped it back into her pocket, peeking back through the curtain. “Why is Alice wearing a bowtie?”

“She has a date,” Rio informed her with a grin. “Didn’t I tell you?”

“No…” Dulce said, her grin widening. “Have you met her yet?”

“Yep,” Rio confirmed. 

“And…? What’s she like?”

“Annoying,” Rio retorted.

Dulce’s brow furrowed. “Really? Then why are you letting this happen?”

“ Letting ?” Rio snorted. “Alice is a grown woman, Dulce. She can date whomever the hell she wants.”

“But then we have to deal with her new girlfriend’s annoying behavior,” Dulce practically whined, pouting at Rio. 

“Eh, you’ll be fine,” Rio laughed, patting her on the cheek. 

Dulce swatted her hand away and peeked back out through the curtain, her eyes widening and her mouth gaping. Immediately, Rio knew that she was there.

And Jen was, too.

“Is that Jennifer fucking Kale!?” Dulce hissed in Rio’s direction. 

“It might be,” Rio said, avoiding her wide-eyed gaze.

“And…Rio?”

“Hmm?” Rio asked, pretending to read through her notes, but suddenly she couldn’t read at all.

“Why the fuck is Agatha Harkness here?”

“She is?” Rio asked, her voice cracking on the second syllable, her cheeks going hot. “Wow, what a small world.”

“She’s sitting in one of our reserved seats in the front,” Dulce pointed out. “Which means that her name was on the list at will-call. Alice is holding Jennifer Kale’s hand, Rio!” 

“Whoa, what a funny random happenstance,” Rio said. 

“RIO, YOU GIVE WILL-CALL ALL THE NAMES!”

“Shhhh!” Rio said, placing her hand over Dulce’s mouth as she heard murmurs from the crowd. She peeked out to see a few faces turned toward the curtains and she immediately pulled them completely closed and tugged Dulce away.

“This is their first date,” Rio said, “and that’s really all I can say about it.”

“Technically, she’s fourth-wheeling,” Rio murmured.

“But, Agatha…” She took a deep, shuddering breath and pulled away, her eyebrows knitted together. “Agatha?”

“Uh…yeah?” Rio replied, confused. “That’s Agatha Harkness, isn’t it?”

“But we’ve never called her by anything but her full name, but you just called her… Agatha .”

“Who has the time to say her full name right now?” Rio huffed, avoiding her gaze. “I have, like, two minutes before the curtains part, babe. Go take your seat and we’ll talk later, okay?”

Dulce narrowed her eyes at her older sister. “Yes,” she said, “we will.”

Rio did not like the tone of her voice, but there was nothing she could do about that now as Dulce was already making her way out the side door and appearing in the crowd, cautiously walking to where Alice sat in the front row. Agatha was dressed just as casually as always, but there was something in the way her lavender blouse hung on her frame under the deep violet cardigan. There was something about the tighter fit of her black jeans and the way her hair fell in much looser curls than usual. Her eyes were lined in black and there was definitely some eyeshadow on her eyelids and a sheen to her lips that spoke of gloss. Rio wondered, briefly, if it was flavored.

She kind of hoped she’d get to find out.

The thought made her heart stutter in her chest as she let the curtains close and she stumbled slightly to her stool. Her leg was tingling and she needed to sit. She perched herself as she waited for the sign that the curtains were about to part. She gripped her cane in front of her for a long moment, her fingers tracing a familiar flower carved into the wood as she steadied her breathing.

Rio Vidal never got stage fright, and she wasn’t about to start now.

 

Agatha Harkness hadn’t been to a lot of comedy shows.

She liked stand-up comedy, sometimes. She liked to watch them on the TV at the gym when it was too icy to run outside while she ran on a treadmill. She preferred female comedians for the most part. 

She never thought she’d be interested in dating one.

Not that she and Rio were dating. This was just a show. She was supporting a friend. 

A hot friend. 

A hot, lesbian friend. 

A hot, lesbian friend who was the subject of a photograph she kept in her studio, where her most prized and beloved art pieces hung.

A hot, lesbian friend who she very nearly kissed earlier on her office floor while they gazed up at glow-in-the-dark stars and hand-painted planets and galaxies while Agatha shared more with Rio than with most people she’d known for much longer.

But that didn’t mean they were dating .

Did it?

No. No, it didn’t, she decided.

It was surrounded by other bars and restaurants on one side of the street and apartments on the other. Agatha was lucky to find a parking space on a Saturday night. There wasn’t much to do in Westview, New Jersey, after all.

Jen was dressed to the nines in a dusty rose pink dress that flared slightly around her knees and showed off her shoulders and collarbones. She had replaced most of her silver piercings with some gold pieces and gave herself a deep red lipstain that made her lips look more plump. Her eyes were outlined in kohl, making them look smoky and mesmerizing – you know, to somebody who might have actually been attracted to her.

“You look like you have two black eyes and a bloody mouth,” Agatha had said when Jen slid into the passenger seat of her orange Subaru Crosstrek, wrinkling her nose. “You sure you wanna go looking like that? You’re probably gonna scare the poor girl off.”

Jen scoffed. “Please,” she said. “Alice grew up around rockstars. This is likely nothing to her.”

“So you’re saying she’ll pretty much be bored by it?” Agatha teased, pulling away from the curb.

“Calm your tits,” she said. “ Jeez! Does Alice know how violent you are?”

“Not yet,” Jen purred.

Agatha wrinkled her nose. “Gross, Jen,” she said. Jen had cackled in response.

The Witches’ Road was wedged between a pizza shop that boasted being open until midnight (which was late for Westview, even on a Saturday night) and some kind of hibachi place with floor-to-ceiling windows. Agatha watched flames jump as a woman shouted something in Japanese while the patrons applauded for her and she bowed. 

“You okay?” Jen asked, nudging Agatha’s shoulder as they walked up the street to the bar. They were only a few feet away when it seemed as though Agatha’s feet got stuck in cement. 

She swallowed thickly and nodded. “Yeah,” she said, “I’m fine. I’m not the one going on a first date with a rockstar’s daughter.” She grinned up at Jen, nudging her side with her elbow.

“Shut up,” she huffed as they continued their walk to the bar’s entrance.
